\section{引言}\label{ux5f15ux8a00}}

本文整合了北大心理与认知科学学院《心理统计 II》2023 春学期所有 13 章 R
实操：

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{期中范围（第 1--7 章）}：非参数检验、ANCOVA、Latin
  Square、Nested ANOVA、MANOVA、Logistic 回归、多元线性回归
\item
  \textbf{期末范围（第 8--13 章）}：调节与中介、相关与距离、PCA /
  EFA、信度分析、生存分析、聚类分析
\end{itemize}

每节配 R 实操代码 + 示例数据 +
完整运行结果（含表格、回归输出、模型诊断图、KM 曲线、聚类树状图等）。

\hypertarget{ux7b2cux4e00ux90e8ux5206ux671fux4e2dux8303ux56f417-ux7ae0}{%
\section{第一部分：期中范围（1--7

\hypertarget{non-parametric-tests}{%
\section{Non-Parametric Tests}\label{non-parametric-tests}}

Parametric statistics assumes data coming from a type of probability
distribution (i.e., the parametric form of the distribution) and makes
inferences about the parameters of the distribution. Estimation is about
estimating the parameters; Hypothesis testing is about deriving the
sampling distribution of a test statistic (e.g., t, F).

Non-parametric statistics uses \textbf{distribution-free} methods which
do not rely on assumptions that the data are drawn from a given
probability distribution (don't rely on the estimation of parameters).

The disadvantage of non-parametric tests is that they're less powerful!
The probability of rejecting the null hypothesis is lower, and also a
higher probability to make type II error when a true effect or
difference exists.

Non-parametric tests are used when:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Non-normality or skewness
\item
  Outliers
\item
  Small sample sizes Thus don't trust normality assumptions
\item
  \textbf{Ordinal} or \textbf{Nominal} data
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{one-sample}{%
\subsection{One Sample}\label{one-sample}}

\hypertarget{chi-square-test}{%
\subsubsection{Chi-Square Test}\label{chi-square-test}}

The chi-square test is a statistical hypothesis test that is used to
determine \textbf{if there is a significant association between two
categorical variables}. It is a non-parametric test, which means that it
does not assume any particular distribution of the data.

The test involves comparing the \emph{observed frequencies} of different
categories with the \emph{expected frequencies}, assuming that there is
no association between the two variables. The expected frequencies are
calculated based on the assumption of \textbf{independence} between the
two variables.

The test statistic is calculated as

\[
\begin{aligned}
\chi^2&=\sum^C\frac{(f_o-f_e)^2}{f_e}\\
C=\#\text{Categories},\ &f_o=\text{observed freq}.,\ f_e=\text{expected freq.}
\end{aligned}
\]

Note that the degree of freedom for chi-square statistic does not depend
on the sample size, but only on number of categories.

The resulting value is then compared to the chi-square distribution. If
the calculated chi-square value is greater than the critical value from
the chi-square distribution, then the null hypothesis of independence is
rejected, and it can be concluded that there is a significant
association between the two variables.

Note that for chi-square test, under each category (bin), the sample
size should be no less than 5, otherwise you have to merge some adjacent
categories or use less bins.

\hypertarget{goodness-of-fit}{%
\paragraph{Goodness of Fit}\label{goodness-of-fit}}

The function takes one or more contingency tables as input and produces
a chi-square test result object that contains the test statistic,
degrees of freedom, p-value, and expected frequencies.

Here is the general syntax for using the chisq.test() function:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{chisq.test}\NormalTok{(x, }\AttributeTok{y =} \ConstantTok{NULL}\NormalTok{, }
           \AttributeTok{correct =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }
           \AttributeTok{p =} \ConstantTok{NULL}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{rescale.p =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The \texttt{x} argument is the contingency table or data that is used to
perform the chi-square test. If \texttt{y} is specified, a test for
independence is performed, and if \texttt{y} is not specified, a
goodness of fit test is performed.

The \texttt{correct} argument is a logical value that indicates whether
to apply a continuity correction to the test statistic. The \texttt{p}
argument specifies the expected probabilities for the goodness of fit
test. The \texttt{rescale.p} argument is a logical value that indicates
whether to rescale the expected probabilities to sum to 1 (if
necessary).

The most useful example for the test of goodness of fit is in linear
models. Suppose with the well-known dataset \texttt{mtcars}, we're to
check the fitting effect between predicted value and the real value,
regressing \texttt{mpg} on \texttt{wt}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{chisq.test}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{fitted}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{lm}\NormalTok{(mpg}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{wt,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ mtcars)),}
\NormalTok{           mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{mpg)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Pearson's Chi-squared test
## 
## data:  fitted(lm(mpg ~ wt, data = mtcars)) and mtcars$mpg
## X-squared = 682.67, df = 672, p-value = 0.3792
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{independece-test}{%
\paragraph{Independece test}\label{independece-test}}

It's beautiful for the logic under the independence test, and its
relationship with test of goodness of fit.

Here, we've successfully \textbf{transformed a problem of correlation
into a problem of the similarity of distributions}. And such
transformation is interesting and not twisted at all.

Suppose we have data on 500 individuals regarding their smoking habits
and whether they have lung cancer. The data is summarized in the
following contingency table:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{smoking }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Never"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Former"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Current"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{cancer }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Yes"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"No"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{observed }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{matrix}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{200}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{),}
                   \AttributeTok{nrow =} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{byrow =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}
                   \AttributeTok{dimnames =} \FunctionTok{list}\NormalTok{(smoking, cancer))}
\NormalTok{observed}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##         Yes  No
## Never    50 200
## Former  100 100
## Current  50   0
\end{verbatim}

In this table, the rows represent the smoking status of the individuals
(\texttt{Never}, \texttt{Former}, \texttt{Current}), and the columns
represent whether they have lung cancer (\texttt{Yes}, \texttt{No}).

Pass a contingency table into the \texttt{chisq.test()} directly and see
the result, testing whether smoking is associated with lung cancer.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{smoke }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{chisq.test}\NormalTok{(observed)}
\NormalTok{smoke}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Pearson's Chi-squared test
## 
## data:  observed
## X-squared = 125, df = 2, p-value < 2.2e-16
\end{verbatim}

Moreover, we can also inspect the \emph{expected} frequencies of the
contingency table using the \texttt{result\$expected} command. This will
give us a matrix of the expected frequencies under the assumption of
independence between smoking and lung cancer.

This will undoubtedly save great efficiency, in case that it's tested by
the teacher, otherwise there's gonna be a huge efficiency loss.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{smoke}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{expected}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##         Yes  No
## Never   100 150
## Former   80 120
## Current  20  30
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{effect-size}{%
\paragraph{Effect Size}\label{effect-size}}

\texttt{rstatix::cramer\_v()} is a function in R that is used to compute
the Cramer's V statistic, which is a measure of the association between
two categorical variables. Cramer's V is a normalized version of the
chi-square statistic and is often used as a measure of effect size in
chi-square tests.

The parameters of this function is much the same as
\texttt{chisq.test()}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{rstatix}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{cramer\_v}\NormalTok{(observed)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.5
\end{verbatim}

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{cramer v}

\hypertarget{binomial-test}{%
\subsubsection{Binomial Test}\label{binomial-test}}

The binomial test is a statistical hypothesis test that is used to
determine \textbf{if the proportion of successes in a binary experiment
is significantly different from a hypothesized value}. The test assumes
that the outcome of each trial is either a success or a failure and that
the trials are independent.

In the binomial test, the null hypothesis is that the proportion of
successes is equal to a hypothesized value, and the alternative
hypothesis is that the proportion of successes is different from the
hypothesized value.

In R, the \texttt{binom.test()} function can be used to perform the
binomial test. Here is an example of how to use the function:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Generate a vector of binary data}
\NormalTok{toss }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Perform the binomial test}
\FunctionTok{binom.test}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(toss),}\FunctionTok{length}\NormalTok{(toss),}\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Exact binomial test
## 
## data:  sum(toss) and length(toss)
## number of successes = 6, number of trials = 10, p-value = 0.7539
## alternative hypothesis: true probability of success is not equal to 0.5
## 95 percent confidence interval:
##  0.2623781 0.8784477
## sample estimates:
## probability of success 
##                    0.6
\end{verbatim}

In this example, we first generate a vector of binary data representing
the outcomes of a binary experiment. We then use the
\texttt{binom.test()} function to perform the binomial test on this
data, specifying the number of successes (\texttt{sum(data)}), the
number of trials (\texttt{length(data)}), and the hypothesized
proportion of successes (\texttt{p\ =\ 0.5}).

The output of the test will include the test statistic, the p-value, and
the confidence interval for the proportion of successes.

\hypertarget{runs-test}{%
\subsubsection{Runs Test}\label{runs-test}}

The runs test is a non-parametric test that can be used to
\textbf{assess the randomness of a set of data}. The test is based on
the number of runs in the data, where a run is defined as a sequence of
consecutive observations with the same sign.

In statistics, a ``run'' is a sequence of consecutive data points that
have the same value or the same sign. A run can be defined for any type
of data, whether it's categorical or continuous.

For example, consider the following sequence of binary data:

1 1 0 1 0 0 0 1 1 1

In this sequence, there are 4 runs: two runs of 1's, one run of 0's, and
one run of 1's.

Another example is a sequence of temperature readings over a period of
time:

20.1 20.3 20.4 20.4 20.5 20.4 20.2 20.0

In this sequence, there is one run of increasing temperatures (from 20.1
to 20.5) followed by one run of decreasing temperatures (from 20.5 to
20.0).

The null hypothesis of the runs test is that the data is random, and the
alternative hypothesis is that the data is not random. The test
statistic is based on the number of runs in the data and is compared to
the expected number of runs under the assumption of randomness.

The expected number of runs can be calculated using the following
formula:

\[
E(R)=\frac{2n_1n_2}{n_1+n_2}+1
\]

where \(n_1\) is the number of positive signs in the data, \(n_2\) is
the number of negative signs in the data.

The test statistic is given by:

\[
z=\frac{R-E(R)}{Var(R)}
\]

where \(R\) is the observed number of runs, \(E(R)\) is the expected
number of runs, and \(Var(R)\) is the variance of the number of runs.
Under the null hypothesis of randomness, the test statistic follows a
standard normal distribution.

Use \texttt{DescTools::RunsTest(data)} in R to determine whether a coin
is randomly tossed or not:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Generate a vector of coin toss outcomes (0 = tails, 1 = heads)}
\NormalTok{coin\_tosses }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }
                 \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Perform the runs test}
\NormalTok{DescTools}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{RunsTest}\NormalTok{(coin\_tosses)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Runs Test for Randomness
## 
## data:  coin_tosses
## runs = 11, m = 7, n = 9, p-value = 0.3024
## alternative hypothesis: true number of runs is not equal the expected number
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{ks-test}{%
\subsubsection{KS Test}\label{ks-test}}

The Kolmogorov-Smirnov (KS) test is a nonparametric statistical test
that is used to compare the distributions of two samples or to test
whether a sample follows a specific distribution. The KS test is based
on the maximum difference between the empirical cumulative distribution
functions (ECDFs) of the two samples.

The KS test can be used to test the null hypothesis that the two samples
are drawn from the same distribution or that a single sample is drawn
from a specific distribution. The test is based on the KS statistic,
which is defined as the maximum vertical difference between the ECDFs of
the two samples.

To perform the KS test in R, you can use the \texttt{ks.test()}
function, which is part of the base R package. Here's the syntax for the
\texttt{ks.test()} function:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{ks.test}\NormalTok{(x, y, alternative, }
        \AttributeTok{exact =} \ConstantTok{NULL}\NormalTok{, }
        \AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.95}\NormalTok{, ...)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

where \texttt{x} and \texttt{y} are the two samples to be compared,
\texttt{alternative} is a character string specifying the alternative
hypothesis (either ``two.sided'', ``less'', or ``greater''),
\texttt{exact} is a logical value indicating whether to use the exact
distribution of the KS statistic (for small sample sizes), and
\texttt{conf.level} is the confidence level for the test.

Here's an example of how to perform the KS test in R using the
\texttt{ks.test()} function:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Generate two samples of data from normal distributions}
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{123}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{x }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rnorm}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{mean =} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{sd =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rnorm}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{mean =} \FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{sd =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Perform the KS test}
\FunctionTok{ks.test}\NormalTok{(x, y)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Asymptotic two-sample Kolmogorov-Smirnov test
## 
## data:  x and y
## D = 0.27, p-value = 0.001365
## alternative hypothesis: two-sided
\end{verbatim}

In this example, we first generate two samples of data from normal
distributions with different means. We then use the \texttt{ks.test()}
function to perform the KS test on the two samples. The function returns
a list containing the test statistic, the p-value, and the alternative
hypothesis.

Moreover, when comparing your data to a specific distribution, you need
to specify the distribution you want to compare your data to using the
\texttt{y} argument, and also provide the parameters of the distribution
using additional arguments \texttt{...}. For example, if you want to
compare your data to a normal distribution, you can provide the mean and
standard deviation of the normal distribution using the mean and sd
arguments, respectively. If you want to compare your data to a uniform
distribution, you can provide the minimum and maximum values of the
uniform distribution using the min and max arguments, respectively.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Generate a sample of data}
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{123}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{x }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rnorm}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{mean =} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{sd =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Perform the KS test against a normal distribution}
\FunctionTok{ks.test}\NormalTok{(x, }\StringTok{"pnorm"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{mean =} \FunctionTok{mean}\NormalTok{(x), }\AttributeTok{sd =} \FunctionTok{sd}\NormalTok{(x))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Asymptotic one-sample Kolmogorov-Smirnov test
## 
## data:  x
## D = 0.058097, p-value = 0.8884
## alternative hypothesis: two-sided
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{kendalls-tau}{%
\subsubsection{Kendall's Tau}\label{kendalls-tau}}

Kendall's tau is a non-parametric measure of association for
\textbf{ordinal} data that quantifies the strength and
\textbf{direction} of the relationship between two variables. Kendall's
tau is particularly useful when the variables being studied are ordinal
or ranked.

Kendall's tau is based on the difference between the number of
concordant and discordant pairs of observations. A concordant pair is a
pair in which the ranks of both variables are in the same direction
(i.e., both are either increasing or decreasing). A discordant pair is a
pair in which the ranks of the variables are in the opposite direction
(i.e., one is increasing while the other is decreasing).

Kendall's tau coefficient ranges from -1 to +1, with values of -1
indicating a perfect negative association, 0 indicating no association,
and +1 indicating a perfect positive association. The formula for
Kendall's tau is:

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{kendall's tau}

In R, you can calculate Kendall's tau using the \texttt{cor.test()}
functionwith the argument \texttt{method\ =\ "kendall"}. The output of
the test provides the estimated Kendall's tau coefficient, along with
the p-value and confidence interval for the test.

In this example, we have ten companies in the tech industry and their
ranks of revenue for two different years.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Company & Year 1 Rank & Year 2 Rank \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Apple & 1 & 2 \\
Microsoft & 2 & 1 \\
Amazon & 3 & 3 \\
Alphabet & 4 & 4 \\
Facebook & 5 & 5 \\
Intel & 6 & 6 \\
IBM & 7 & 7 \\
Samsung & 8 & 9 \\
Cisco & 9 & 8 \\
Qualcomm & 10 & 10 \\
\end{longtable}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{rank\_data }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{Company =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Apple"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Microsoft"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Amazon"}\NormalTok{, }
              \StringTok{"Alphabet"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Facebook"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Intel"}\NormalTok{, }
              \StringTok{"IBM"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Samsung"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Cisco"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Qualcomm"}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{Year\_1\_Rank =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{Year\_2\_Rank =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{cor.test}\NormalTok{(rank\_data}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{Year\_1\_Rank, }
\NormalTok{         rank\_data}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{Year\_2\_Rank, }
         \AttributeTok{method =} \StringTok{"kendall"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Kendall's rank correlation tau
## 
## data:  rank_data$Year_1_Rank and rank_data$Year_2_Rank
## T = 43, p-value = 2.976e-05
## alternative hypothesis: true tau is not equal to 0
## sample estimates:
##       tau 
## 0.9111111
\end{verbatim}

The high tau and highly significant result indicate that there's a
innegligible great relationship.

\hypertarget{kendalls-w}{%
\subsubsection{Kendall's W}\label{kendalls-w}}

Kendall's W is a measure of agreement among multiple raters or judges
who are assessing the same set of items or objects. Kendall's W is based
on the number of concordant and discordant triples of ratings among the
raters.

Kendall's W ranges from 0 to 1, with values of 0 indicating no agreement
among the raters and 1 indicating perfect agreement. The formula for
Kendall's W is:

\[
W = (n_c - n_d) / (n_c + n_d + n_t)
\] where \(n_c\) is the number of concordant triples, \(n_d\) is the
number of discordant triples, and \(n_t\) is the number of tied triples.

In another form,

\[
W=\frac{\sum_iR_i^2-\frac{(\sum_iR_i)^2}{N}}{\frac1{12}M^2(N^3-N)}
\]

where \(R_i\) is the total ranking of \emph{i}th subject.

Kendall's W can be converted into Spearman correlation.

\[
r_{spearman}=\frac{MW-1}{M-1}
\]

In R, you can calculate Kendall's W using the
\texttt{irr::kendall(ratings)} function, where rating is a \(n*m\)
matrix, \(n\) subjects and \(m\) raters.

Here's an example of how to use the \texttt{irr::kendall()} function to
calculate Kendall's W coefficient:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Create a data frame with ratings from three judges for four items}
\NormalTok{ratings }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{Item =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"A"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"C"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"D"}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{Judge\_1 =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{Judge\_2 =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{Judge\_3 =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{))}

\NormalTok{irr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{kendall}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{ratings =}\NormalTok{ ratings)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##  Kendall's coefficient of concordance W
## 
##  Subjects = 4 
##    Raters = 4 
##         W = 0.225 
## 
##  Chisq(3) = 2.7 
##   p-value = 0.44
\end{verbatim}

Meanwhile, a chi-square test is carried for each subject according to
their sum of rankings. If significant, there exists a difference between
their performance.

Note that there's no signal about the significance about W, only that
about \(\chi^2\).

\hypertarget{two-samples}{%
\subsection{Two Samples}\label{two-samples}}

\hypertarget{related-samples}{%
\subsubsection{Related Samples}\label{related-samples}}

\hypertarget{wilcoxon-signed-ranks-test}{%
\paragraph{Wilcoxon Signed Ranks
Test}\label{wilcoxon-signed-ranks-test}}

The Wilcoxon Signed Ranks test is a nonparametric statistical test used
to determine whether the \textbf{median} of a paired difference between
two related samples is statistically different from zero. It is used
when the data does not meet the assumptions of normality required for a
parametric test like the paired t-test.

The test is performed by first calculating the differences between the
paired observations, then ranking the absolute values of these
differences. The sum of the ranks of the positive differences is then
compared to the sum of the ranks of the negative differences, and take
the smaller one as the statistic called \(T\). The null hypothesis is
that the median difference is zero, while the alternative hypothesis is
that it is not zero.

In R, you can perform the Wilcoxon Signed Ranks test using the
\texttt{wilcox.test()} function. \texttt{wilcox.test()} is a function in
R that performs the \textbf{Wilcoxon rank test}, or the
\textbf{Mann-Whitney U test}. When conducting the former,
\texttt{paired\ =\ T}; the latter, \texttt{paired\ =\ F}. Just specify
the two vectors as the first two parameters.

Here's an example of how to use the \texttt{wilcox.test()} function to
perform the test on two related samples. Suppose we are interested in
comparing the effectiveness of two different weight loss programs. We
have a sample of 10 individuals who have completed both programs. We
measure each individual's weight before and after each program, and we
want to determine whether there is a significant difference in weight
loss between the two programs.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Create two related samples}
\NormalTok{weightloss }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{individual =} \DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{,}
  \AttributeTok{program\_A =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{185}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{190}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{200}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{175}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{195}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{180}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{170}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{185}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{195}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{200}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{program\_B =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{180}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{185}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{195}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{170}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{190}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{175}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{165}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{180}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{190}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{195}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Perform the Wilcoxon Signed Ranks test}
\FunctionTok{wilcox.test}\NormalTok{(weightloss}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{program\_A,weightloss}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{program\_B, }\AttributeTok{paired =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Wilcoxon signed rank test with continuity correction
## 
## data:  weightloss$program_A and weightloss$program_B
## V = 55, p-value = 0.001904
## alternative hypothesis: true location shift is not equal to 0
\end{verbatim}

Note:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Faced with tied values, typically use the averge rank.
\item
  Faced with zero difference, typically keep the observation. Deleting
  data is always a concern! Zero difference observation can participate
  in computing \(T\) and influence the degree of freedom.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{mcnemar-test}{%
\paragraph{McNemar Test}\label{mcnemar-test}}

In the context of the McNemar Test, the off-diagonal terms refer to the
counts of the two categories that are inconsistent between the two
paired measurements. Specifically, they refer to the counts of the
following two categories:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  False positives (FP): The number of cases where the first measurement
  is negative, but the second measurement is positive.
\item
  False negatives (FN): The number of cases where the first measurement
  is positive, but the second measurement is negative.
\end{itemize}

For example, consider a medical study that measures the presence or
absence of a certain disease in a group of patients before and after
treatment. The 2x2 contingency table for this study might look like
this:

\begin{verbatim}
           After Treatment
           Positive Negative
Before Treatment
Positive      a       b
Negative      c       d
\end{verbatim}

In this table, \texttt{a} represents the number of patients who tested
positive for the disease both before and after treatment (true
positives), \texttt{d} represents the number of patients who tested
negative for the disease both before and after treatment (true
negatives), \texttt{b} represents the number of patients who tested
negative for the disease before treatment but positive after treatment
(false positives), and \texttt{c} represents the number of patients who
tested positive for the disease before treatment but negative after
treatment (false negatives).

The off-diagonal terms in the McNemar Test are used because they
represent the counts of the inconsistent categories between two paired
measurements. These inconsistent categories are the false positives (FP)
and false negatives (FN), which represent the cases where the two
measurements disagree.

In the context of the McNemar Test, the goal is to determine whether
there is a significant difference between the proportions of the
inconsistent categories, namely FP and FN. This is done by comparing the
observed counts of FP and FN to the expected counts under the null
hypothesis of no difference. The expected counts are calculated assuming
that the marginal totals of the contingency table are fixed, which is a
characteristic of paired data.

So the key difference between the Chi-Square Test on a Contingency Table
and the McNemar Test is the question that each test addresses. The
former tests for independence between two categorical variables, while
the latter tests for a significant difference between two correlated
proportions. The two tests have different assumptions and are used in
different contexts, but both use the chi-squared statistic to calculate
the test statistic.

Here's an example of the McNemar Test being used to analyze a medical
experiment in R. In this example, we will examine the effectiveness of a
new treatment for a certain disease compared to a standard treatment.

Suppose we have a sample of 50 patients who have been diagnosed with the
disease. We randomly assign each patient to receive either the new
treatment (Treatment A) or the standard treatment (Treatment B). After
the treatment, we measure whether the patient's symptoms have improved
or not. The results are summarized in the following 2x2 contingency
table:

\begin{verbatim}
           After Treatment
           Improved Not Improved
Before Treatment
Improved        15        10
Not Improved    5        20
\end{verbatim}

In this table, the rows represent the before-treatment condition and the
columns represent the after-treatment condition. The cells represent the
number of patients in each category. For example, 15 patients improved
after receiving Treatment A, while 10 did not improve.

To analyze these data using the McNemar Test in R, we can use the
\texttt{mcnemar.test()} function. Here's the R code:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Create a 2x2 contingency table of the data}
\NormalTok{medicine }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{matrix}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{), }
               \AttributeTok{nrow =} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }
               \AttributeTok{dimnames =} \FunctionTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Improved"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Not Improved"}\NormalTok{), }
                               \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Improved"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Not Improved"}\NormalTok{)))}

\CommentTok{\# Perform the McNemar Test}
\FunctionTok{mcnemar.test}\NormalTok{(medicine)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  McNemar's Chi-squared test with continuity correction
## 
## data:  medicine
## McNemar's chi-squared = 1.0667, df = 1, p-value = 0.3017
\end{verbatim}

In this code, we first create a 2x2 contingency table of the data using
the \texttt{matrix()} function. We then use the \texttt{mcnemar.test()}
function with the contingency table as the input. The function returns a
list of information about the test, including the chi-squared statistic,
the degrees of freedom, and the p-value.

In the example above, we cannot reject the null hypothesis that there is
no significant difference between the proportions of patients who
improved after receiving Treatment A compared to Treatment B. McNemar
test is used to compare the proportions of patients who improved after
receiving a new treatment compared to a standard treatment. The output
of the test provides information about the significance of the
difference between the proportions, which can inform decisions about the
effectiveness of the treatments.

\hypertarget{sign-test}{%
\paragraph{Sign Test}\label{sign-test}}

The Sign Test is a non-parametric statistical test that is used to
compare two related samples or matched pairs in which the data are
measured on an ordinal or continuous scale. The test is based on the
distribution of the signs of the differences between the paired
observations.

The Sign Test is used when the data do not meet the assumptions of a
parametric test, such as the paired t-test, which assumes that the data
are normally distributed. The Sign Test is more robust to outliers and
non-normality, and it does not require knowledge of the distribution of
the data.

The Sign Test is a one-sample test, meaning that it compares the
differences between the paired observations to a specified value, such
as zero. The null hypothesis is that the median difference between the
paired observations is equal to zero, while the alternative hypothesis
is that the median difference is not equal to zero.

To perform the Sign Test, we first calculate the differences between the
paired observations. We then assign a sign (+ or -) to each difference,
depending on whether the observation in the first sample is greater or
less than the observation in the second sample. We then count the number
of positive and negative signs, and calculate the test statistic as the
minimum of the two counts. The test statistic follows a binomial
distribution with the number of pairs as the sample size and a
probability of 0.5 under the null hypothesis.

The p-value of the test can be calculated using the \textbf{binomial}
distribution, or it can be \emph{approximated} using a \emph{normal}
approximation. If the p-value is less than the chosen significance
level, the null hypothesis is rejected, and the conclusion is that there
is a significant difference between the two related samples.

The Sign Test can be useful in a variety of applications, including
clinical trials, psychology experiments, and ecological studies. It is a
simple and robust method for comparing two related samples when the data
do not meet the assumptions of a parametric test.

There's no \texttt{sign.test()} in R, but that can be accomplished
``manually'' through \texttt{binomial.test()}, since the statistic
follows such distribution. We only have to assign the signs of positive
or negative according to the differences.

Same with the ``weightloss'' example in the Wilcoxon test, we'll
continue that in the sign test.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{binom.test}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =} \FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(weightloss}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{program\_A }\SpecialCharTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ weightloss}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{program\_B),}
               \AttributeTok{n =} \FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(weightloss))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Exact binomial test
## 
## data:  sum(weightloss$program_A > weightloss$program_B) and nrow(weightloss)
## number of successes = 10, number of trials = 10, p-value = 0.001953
## alternative hypothesis: true probability of success is not equal to 0.5
## 95 percent confidence interval:
##  0.6915029 1.0000000
## sample estimates:
## probability of success 
##                      1
\end{verbatim}

Note that the tests for signed ranks of related samples may vary a lot
in terms of their significance level, which largely indicates that the
effectiveness of nonparametric tests may be limited. However, the
\textbf{Wilcoxon test} is generally more recognized.

\hypertarget{independent-samples}{%
\subsubsection{Independent Samples}\label{independent-samples}}

\hypertarget{mann-whitney-u-test}{%
\paragraph{Mann-Whitney U Test}\label{mann-whitney-u-test}}

The Mann-Whitney U Test, is a non-parametric statistical test that is
used to compare the \textbf{medians} of two \textbf{independent}
samples.

The Mann-Whitney U Test is based on the rank sums of the two samples. To
perform the Mann-Whitney U Test, we first combine the two samples and
rank the observations from lowest to highest, assigning the same rank to
ties. We then calculate the sum of the ranks for each sample. The test
statistic is then calculated as the smaller of the two rank sums, \(U\).
The test statistic follows a normal distribution with a mean and
variance that can be calculated from the sample sizes and the \(U\)
statistic. Below is an example.

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{Mann-Whitney U Rank}

The p-value of the test can be calculated using the normal distribution,
or it can be approximated using tables or software. If the p-value is
\textbf{less} than the chosen significance level, the null hypothesis is
rejected, and the conclusion is that there is a significant difference
between the two independent samples.

It is worth noting that the Mann-Whitney U Test is different from the
Wilcoxon Signed-Rank Test. \textbf{The Mann-Whitney U Test is used for
independent samples, while the Wilcoxon Signed-Rank Test is used for
paired samples.}

Here, we assume that in the example provided in the ``weightloss'' part,
the two data in two programs correspond to two groups of people, and
thus independent.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{wilcox.test}\NormalTok{(weightloss}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{program\_A,}
\NormalTok{            weightloss}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{program\_B,}
            \AttributeTok{paired =}\NormalTok{ F)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Wilcoxon rank sum test with continuity correction
## 
## data:  weightloss$program_A and weightloss$program_B
## W = 64, p-value = 0.3029
## alternative hypothesis: true location shift is not equal to 0
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{ks-z-test}{%
\paragraph{KS Z Test}\label{ks-z-test}}

Compared to KS test before, this is a more ``general'' version. KS Z
test compares two distributions against each other, while KS test,
strictly speaking, compares a distribution against a theoretical
distribution.

Use the same function \texttt{ks.test()} as before, all others the same,
so \emph{no} need to cover it again here.

Moreover, it's interesting to compare KS test and Mann-Whitney U test.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The Mann-Whitney test first ranks all the values from low to high, and
  then computes a P value that depends on the discrepancy between the
  mean ranks of the two groups.
\item
  The Kolmogorov-Smirnov test compares the cumulative distribution of
  the two data sets, and computes a \(p\) value that depends on the
  largest.
\end{itemize}

Guidelines for choosing between the two tests.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The KS test is sensitive to any differences in the two distributions.
  Substantial differences in shape, spread or median will result in a
  small P value. In contrast, the \textbf{MW test is mostly sensitive to
  changes in the median}.
\item
  The MW test is used more often and is recognized by more people, so
  choose it if you have no idea which to choose.
\item
  The MW test has been extended to handle tied values. The KS test does
  not handle ties so well. If your data are categorical, so has many
  ties, don't choose the KS test.
\item
  Some fields of science tend to prefer the KS test over the MW test. It
  makes sense to follow the traditions of your field.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{moses-extreme-reaction}{%
\paragraph{Moses Extreme Reaction}\label{moses-extreme-reaction}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Check the span of the rank of one group when two groups are mixed and
  ranked (up and low 5\% deleted).
\item
  \textbf{Good for distributions with mass on both ends}.

  But less powerful and robust as KS test.
\end{itemize}

In the case of MER, the null hypothesis is that there is no difference
in gene expression levels between individuals with extreme reactions and
those with normal reactions. The alternative hypothesis is that there is
a significant difference in gene expression levels between the two
groups.

The \textbf{range} is tested, so the MER has different
\emph{sensitivity} for different distributions.

Use \texttt{DescTools::MosesTest(x,y,extreme)} to carry the MER. If with
a grouping variable, parameters can be passed through a formula-pattern.
\texttt{extreme} allows you to rule out some extreme values.

In the example provided below, the data record the effect of two kinds
of pills. \texttt{ycss} is the effect, and \texttt{zb} is the grouping
label.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{hypnotic }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ haven}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{read\_sav}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Data12{-}06.sav"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The MER is especially useful for medical experiments.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{DescTools}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{MosesTest}\NormalTok{(ycss}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{zb,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ hypnotic)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Moses Test of Extreme Reactions
## 
## data:  ycss by zb
## S = 15, p-value = 0.6858
## alternative hypothesis: extreme values are more likely in x than in y
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{wald-wolfowitz-runs}{%
\paragraph{Wald-Wolfowitz Runs}\label{wald-wolfowitz-runs}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Put two groups together and rank them; the group label should mix if
  they are similar.
\item
  \textbf{Good for distributions that differ more on variance than on
  location}.
\end{itemize}

Wald-Wolfowitz Runs is kind of Runs test, but the two-independent-sample
version.

Use the same function as before, \texttt{DescTools::RunsTest(x,y)} to
carry the test!

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{DescTools}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{RunsTest}\NormalTok{(ycss}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{zb,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ hypnotic)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Wald-Wolfowitz Runs Test
## 
## data:  ycss by zb
## runs = 6, m = 10, n = 10, p-value = 0.03704
## alternative hypothesis: true number of runs is not equal the expected number
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{k-samples}{%
\subsection{K Samples}\label{k-samples}}

\hypertarget{dependent-samples}{%
\subsubsection{Dependent Samples}\label{dependent-samples}}

The ``dependent'' here means, a person or an item is tested for \(K\)
times.

\hypertarget{friedman-test}{%
\paragraph{Friedman Test}\label{friedman-test}}

The Friedman test is a non-parametric statistical test used to determine
whether there are \textbf{differences} among two or more groups based on
\textbf{repeated} measures data. It is often used as an alternative to
\emph{repeated measures ANOVA} when the data do not meet the assumptions
of normality or homogeneity of variance.

The Friedman test involves \emph{ranking} the data for each group and
then computing the \emph{sum of the ranks} for each condition or
treatment. The test statistic is calculated as the ratio of the sum of
squares of the ranks to the total sum of squares, and is compared to the
\emph{chi-squared} distribution with degrees of freedom equal to the
number of groups minus one. The Friedman test is appropriate when the
data are measured on an ordinal or continuous scale, but not when the
data are nominal.

If the test statistic is significant, it indicates that there are
differences among the groups. However, the Friedman test does not
identify which groups are different from each other. \emph{Post-hoc
tests}, such as the Wilcoxon signed-rank test or the Dunn's test, can be
used to identify the pairwise differences between the groups.

One of the advantages of the Friedman test is that it is robust, meaning
that it is not affected by outliers or non-normality in the data. It is
also useful for analyzing repeated measures data, where each participant
or object is tested multiple times under different conditions or
treatments.

The statistic is what we calculated as \(\chi^2\) in Kendall's W. No
need to cover here!

Note that no need to pass the pre-ranked data into \texttt{kendall()},
the raw data is fine!

\hypertarget{cochrans-q}{%
\paragraph{Cochran's Q}\label{cochrans-q}}

Cochran's Q test is a non-parametric statistical test used to analyze
the differences between \emph{three or more} \textbf{related}
categorical variables. It is similar to the McNemar test, which is used
to compare two related categorical variables.

The Cochran's Q test is used when we have a set of subjects measured on
a \textbf{binary} (dichotomous) variable at three or more time points or
under three or more conditions. The test determines if there is a
statistically significant difference in the proportion of positive
responses across the different time points or conditions.

The null hypothesis of the Cochran's Q test is that the proportion of
positive responses is the same across all time points or conditions,
while the alternative hypothesis is that at least one time point or
condition has a different proportion of positive responses.

If the Cochran's Q test indicates that there are significant differences
between the time points or conditions, post-hoc tests such as McNemar's
test or Bonferroni correction can be used to determine which time points
or conditions are significantly different from each other.

Suppose we have a courses and a group of 30 students enrolled. We
measure the pass/fail status of each student at three different time
points (midterm, final exam, and overall course grade) to determine if
there is a significant difference in the proportion of passing students
across the different time points.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{midterm }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{final }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{overall }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pass"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fail"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{))}

\NormalTok{df }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(midterm, final, overall) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} 
  \FunctionTok{gather}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{key =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}exam\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{value =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}result\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}
  \FunctionTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{stu =} \FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{),}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In this dataset, we have three categorical variables (midterm, final,
and overall) measured at three time points for each student. To perform
Cochran's Q test on this dataset, we can use the
\texttt{cochran\_qtest()} function from the \texttt{rstatix} package in
R:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{rstatix}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{cochran\_qtest}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ df,}\AttributeTok{formula =}\NormalTok{ result}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{exam}\SpecialCharTok{|}\NormalTok{stu)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 1 x 6
##   .y.        n statistic    df     p method          
## * <chr>  <int>     <dbl> <dbl> <dbl> <chr>           
## 1 result    30      4.57     2 0.102 Cochran's Q test
\end{verbatim}

The non-significant result here means that the three exams does not have
differences in difficulty.

\hypertarget{kendalls-w-1}{%
\paragraph{Kendall's W}\label{kendalls-w-1}}

In essence, it's a Friedman Test, no need to cover here.

\hypertarget{independent-samples-1}{%
\subsubsection{Independent Samples}\label{independent-samples-1}}

\hypertarget{kruskalwallis-test}{%
\paragraph{Kruskal--Wallis Test}\label{kruskalwallis-test}}

The Kruskal-Wallis test is a non-parametric statistical test used to
compare \emph{three or more} \textbf{independent} samples to determine
if they come from the same population or not. It is often used as an
alternative to \emph{one-way ANOVA} when the data do not meet the
assumptions of normality or homogeneity of variance.

The Kruskal-Wallis test ranks the data within each group and calculates
the sum of ranks for each group. It then uses these sums to calculate a
test statistic that measures the differences between groups. The null
hypothesis of the Kruskal-Wallis test is that the samples come from the
same population, while the alternative hypothesis is that at least one
sample comes from a different population.

The test is appropriate when the data are measured on an ordinal or
continuous scale, but not when the data are nominal. If the
Kruskal-Wallis test indicates that there are significant differences
between the groups, post-hoc tests such as the Mann-Whitney U test or
Dunn's test can be used to determine which groups are significantly
different from each other.

To perform the Kruskal-Wallis test in R, we can use the
\texttt{kruskal.test()} function. Here's an example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{group1 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{group2 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{group3 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{)}

\FunctionTok{kruskal.test}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{list}\NormalTok{(group1, group2, group3))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Kruskal-Wallis rank sum test
## 
## data:  list(group1, group2, group3)
## Kruskal-Wallis chi-squared = 3.2051, df = 2, p-value = 0.2014
\end{verbatim}

In this example, we have three independent groups with five observations
each. We use the \texttt{list()} function to group the data together,
and then pass the list to the \texttt{kruskal.test()} function. The
output of this code will provide the Kruskal-Wallis test statistic,
degrees of freedom, and p-value.

If the data is consisted of one value column and one group column, then
using the formula-pattern would be more convenient!

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{df }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{value =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(group1,group2,group3),}
  \AttributeTok{group =} \FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{each =} \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}

\FunctionTok{kruskal.test}\NormalTok{(value}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{group,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Kruskal-Wallis rank sum test
## 
## data:  value by group
## Kruskal-Wallis chi-squared = 3.2051, df = 2, p-value = 0.2014
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{jonckheere-terpstra-trend-test}{%
\paragraph{Jonckheere-Terpstra (Trend)
test}\label{jonckheere-terpstra-trend-test}}

The Jonckheere-Terpstra test, also known as the trend test, is a
non-parametric statistical test used to determine \textbf{if there is a
trend in the ordered levels of a variable across multiple groups}. It is
often used to analyze data where there is an \textbf{ordered}
categorical variable and the groups are ordered in a natural way.

The Jonckheere-Terpstra test ranks the data within each group and
calculates the sum of ranks for each group. It then uses these sums to
calculate a test statistic that measures the trend in the ordered levels
of the variable. The null hypothesis of the Jonckheere-Terpstra test is
that there is no trend in the ordered levels of the variable across the
groups, while the alternative hypothesis is that there is a trend.

The test is appropriate when the data are measured on an ordinal or
continuous scale, but not when the data are nominal. It is commonly used
in fields such as medicine, psychology, and social sciences to analyze
trends in disease severity, performance, or attitudes across different
groups.

If the Jonckheere-Terpstra test indicates that there is a trend in the
ordered levels of the variable, post-hoc tests such as the Wilcoxon
rank-sum test or Dunn's test can be used to determine which groups are
significantly different from each other.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Only used when the groups are ordered.
\item
  It actually tests about the median between groups.
\item
  it has \textbf{more power} than Kruskal-Wallis.
\end{itemize}

Suppose we want to analyze the trend in intelligence scores across
different age groups. We have four age groups (20-30, 31-40, 41-50, and
51-60) and 10 participants in each group. We measure the intelligence
scores of each participant and want to determine if there is a
significant trend in intelligence scores across the age groups.

Use \texttt{DescTools::JonckheereTerpstraTest()} to carry the test.
\texttt{nperm} is the number of permutations for the reference
distribution. The default is NULL in which case the permutation p-value
is not computed. It's recommended to set \texttt{nperm} to 1000 or
higher if permutation p-value is desired.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{age }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"20{-}30"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"31{-}40"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{), }
         \FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"41{-}50"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"51{-}60"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{age }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{as.ordered}\NormalTok{(age)}
\NormalTok{intelligence }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{85}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{85}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{65}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{,}
                   \DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{85}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{85}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{65}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{,}
                   \DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{85}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{65}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{85}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{,}
                   \DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{65}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{85}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{df }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(age, intelligence)}

\NormalTok{DescTools}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{JonckheereTerpstraTest}\NormalTok{(intelligence}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{age,}
                                  \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Warning in JonckheereTerpstraTest.default(c(80, 75, 85, 90, 70, 75, 80, : Sample size > 100 or data with ties 
##  p-value based on normal approximation. Specify nperm for permutation p-value
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Jonckheere-Terpstra test
## 
## data:  intelligence by age
## JT = 276, p-value = 0.5624
## alternative hypothesis: two.sided
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{median-test}{%
\paragraph{Median Test}\label{median-test}}

Very simple.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Count the \# of data \textgreater{} or \textless{} median;
\item
  A Chi-square test on binomial distribution.
\item
  Need a large sample; not preferred.
\end{itemize}

Now that you've ranked your data, why not try other more powerful
non-parametric tests?

\end{Highlighting}

\hypertarget{non-parametric-correlation-analysis}{%
\subsection{Non-Parametric Correlation
Analysis}\label{non-parametric-correlation-analysis}}

There are several types of correlation coefficients that are commonly
used in statistics to measure the strength and direction of the
relationship between two variables. The most commonly used correlation
coefficients are Pearson correlation, Spearman correlation,
point-biserial correlation, and phi correlation.

Spearman correlation, point-biserial correlation, and phi correlation
are non-parametric ones.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Pearson correlation: Pearson correlation coefficient measures the
  linear relationship between two continuous variables. It ranges from
  -1 to 1, where -1 indicates a perfect negative relationship, 0
  indicates no relationship, and 1 indicates a perfect positive
  relationship. Pearson correlation assumes that the data are normally
  distributed and that there is a linear relationship between the
  variables.
\item
  Spearman correlation: Spearman correlation coefficient measures the
  strength and direction of the monotonic relationship between two
  variables. It ranges from -1 to 1, where -1 indicates a perfect
  negative monotonic relationship, 0 indicates no monotonic
  relationship, and 1 indicates a perfect positive monotonic
  relationship. Spearman correlation does not assume that the data are
  normally distributed and can be used for ordinal or continuous
  variables.
\item
  Point-biserial correlation: Point-biserial correlation coefficient
  measures the relationship between a dichotomous variable and a
  continuous variable. It ranges from -1 to 1, where -1 indicates a
  perfect negative relationship between the dichotomous variable and the
  continuous variable, 0 indicates no relationship, and 1 indicates a
  perfect positive relationship. Point-biserial correlation is used when
  one of the variables is dichotomous and the other is continuous.
\item
  Phi correlation: Phi correlation coefficient measures the relationship
  between two dichotomous variables. It ranges from -1 to 1, where -1
  indicates a perfect negative relationship between the two variables, 0
  indicates no relationship, and 1 indicates a perfect positive
  relationship. Phi correlation is used when both variables are
  dichotomous.
\end{enumerate}

In general, Pearson and Spearman correlations are used for continuous or
ordinal variables, while point-biserial and phi correlations are used
for dichotomous variables. Pearson correlation is most appropriate when
the data are normally distributed and there is a linear relationship
between the variables, while Spearman correlation is more appropriate
when the data are non-normally distributed or the relationship is not
linear. Point-biserial and phi correlations are appropriate when one or
both of the variables are dichotomous.

Moreover, the chi-square independence test is used to determine if there
is a significant association between two categorical variables, while
the phi correlation coefficient is used to measure the strength and
direction of the relationship between two dichotomous variables. The two
tests are different perspectives to view the same problem, either say
correlation or say independence.

All can be dealt with by \texttt{cor.test()}.

What I want to address here is the Phi coefficient. Phi coefficient can
be obtained by \texttt{cor.test()} using the traditional Pearson-way,
and also fine with \texttt{psych::phi(matrix)}, but the latter won't
give you the significance information.

In the example, we want to test the correlation between parents'
political stance and their parenting way. We have the following
contingency table.

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{contingency table}

I'd like to illustrate first with the ``traditional'' way.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{phidt }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{parenting =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{),}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{)),}
  \AttributeTok{politics =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{),}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{),}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{),}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{cor.test}\NormalTok{(phidt}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{parenting,phidt}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{politics)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Pearson's product-moment correlation
## 
## data:  phidt$parenting and phidt$politics
## t = 1.6475, df = 38, p-value = 0.1077
## alternative hypothesis: true correlation is not equal to 0
## 95 percent confidence interval:
##  -0.05797333  0.52729702
## sample estimates:
##       cor 
## 0.2581989
\end{verbatim}

The \texttt{psych::phi(matrix)} is indeed not recommend! But it's last
advantage is that the data is expressed in the easiest way! If just to
get the correlation value, probably go with it.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{phi\_dataset }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{matrix}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{),}\AttributeTok{nrow=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{ncol=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{psych}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{phi}\NormalTok{(phi\_dataset)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.26
\end{verbatim}

However, if we carry a chi-square test, that differs a little in its
significance.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{chisq.test}\NormalTok{(phi\_dataset)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Pearson's Chi-squared test with Yates' continuity correction
## 
## data:  phi_dataset
## X-squared = 1.7067, df = 1, p-value = 0.1914
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{ancova}{%

as \texttt{vitalcp}, an indicator for their health of lungs; their age
as \texttt{age}, probability related to \texttt{vitalcp}; a dummy
\texttt{time} telling whether or not they have worked as a miner for 10
years, 1 if so, 0 otherwise.

\hypertarget{ancova-1}{%
\subsection{ANCOVA}\label{ancova-1}}

\hypertarget{fit-the-model}{%
\subsubsection{Fit the Model}\label{fit-the-model}}

It's noteworthy that in R, the more essential the effect variable is,
the earlier it should be put.

The common ``rank'' is as follows.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  covariate
\item
  main effect
\item
  interaction
\end{itemize}

The order that IVs are plugged matters! Since we have the algorithm of
SS related to that.

In the formula, connect the main effect and covariate with sign
\texttt{+}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cfit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{aov}\NormalTok{(vitalcp}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{age}\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{time,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ miner)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{summary-the-model}{%
\subsubsection{Summary the Model}\label{summary-the-model}}

\hypertarget{ancova-view}{%
\paragraph{ANCOVA View}\label{ancova-view}}

As for the fitted ANCOVA model, say \texttt{model}, plug in
\texttt{model} into \texttt{summary()} or \texttt{anova()} will return
the same and necessary results.

Since \texttt{summary()} is more generally used, I'd like to recommend
\texttt{summary()}!

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# anova(cfit)}
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(cfit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##             Df Sum Sq Mean Sq F value   Pr(>F)    
## age          1 10.543  10.543  19.162 0.000187 ***
## time         1  0.542   0.542   0.985 0.330439    
## Residuals   25 13.755   0.550                     
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{linear-model-view}{%
\paragraph{Linear-Model View}\label{linear-model-view}}

View the ANOVA model as a special case of linear model, with covariate
and main effect as its independent variables as is used above, and
intercept is included.

\texttt{car::Anova(model,type\ =\ \textquotesingle{}III\textquotesingle{})}
can return the SS, miner, \(F\) and \(p\) for each term, including the
intercept. The coefficients, together with the same significance, can be
reported through \texttt{summary(model)}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{car}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{Anova}\NormalTok{(cfit,}\AttributeTok{type =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Anova Table (Type III tests)
## 
## Response: vitalcp
##             Sum Sq Df F value    Pr(>F)    
## (Intercept) 37.959  1 68.9886 1.177e-08 ***
## age         10.881  1 19.7751 0.0001563 ***
## time         0.542  1  0.9852 0.3304389    
## Residuals   13.755 25                      
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{lm}\NormalTok{(vitalcp}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{age}\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{time,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ miner))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Call:
## lm(formula = vitalcp ~ age + time, data = miner)
## 
## Residuals:
##      Min       1Q   Median       3Q      Max 
## -1.68343 -0.37405  0.03523  0.44561  1.12263 
## 
## Coefficients:
##             Estimate Std. Error t value Pr(>|t|)    
## (Intercept)  8.27718    0.99654   8.306 1.18e-08 ***
## age         -0.08700    0.01956  -4.447 0.000156 ***
## time2       -0.30033    0.30258  -0.993 0.330439    
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
## 
## Residual standard error: 0.7418 on 25 degrees of freedom
## Multiple R-squared:  0.4463, Adjusted R-squared:  0.402 
## F-statistic: 10.07 on 2 and 25 DF,  p-value: 0.0006186
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{effect-size-1}{%
\subsubsection{Effect Size}\label{effect-size-1}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{rstatix}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{partial\_eta\_squared}\NormalTok{(cfit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##        age       time 
## 0.43389844 0.03791233
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{post-hoc-test}{%
\subsubsection{Post-Hoc Test}\label{post-hoc-test}}

Library the package \texttt{multcomp} and use the
\texttt{glht(model,\ linfct\ =\ mcp(time\ =\ \textquotesingle{}Tukey\textquotesingle{}))}
within to get the post-hoc model. Then, use \texttt{summary()} to get
its result.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(multcomp)}
\NormalTok{postHoc }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{glht}\NormalTok{(cfit,}\AttributeTok{linfct =} \FunctionTok{mcp}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{time =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Tukey\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(postHoc)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Well, the significant intercept term means that the grand mean does
account for some of the variation.

\hypertarget{visualize-the-model}{%
\subsubsection{Visualize the Model}\label{visualize-the-model}}

Library package \texttt{HH}, and then use the function \texttt{ancova()}
to fit the model. Note that all parameters passed into \texttt{ancova()}
is the same as those into \texttt{aov()}.

Mind the order of independent variables, since that does matter!

\texttt{ancova(...)} will not only return the completely same result as
we've seen in \texttt{summary(aov(...))}, but also a graph illustrating
the comprehensive correlation between the dependent variable and
covariate \& main effect.

Note that within \texttt{ancova()}, more underlying functions from that
package \texttt{HH} are loaded, which means you can not get away with
just specifying the package like \texttt{HH::ancova}, but have to
library the package specifically.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(HH)}
\FunctionTok{ancova}\NormalTok{(vitalcp}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{age}\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{time , }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ miner)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Analysis of Variance Table
## 
## Response: vitalcp
##           Df  Sum Sq Mean Sq F value    Pr(>F)    
## age        1 10.5431 10.5431 19.1617 0.0001871 ***
## time       1  0.5421  0.5421  0.9852 0.3304389    
## Residuals 25 13.7555  0.5502                      
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-45-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{visual-check}{%
\subsubsection{Visual Check}\label{visual-check}}

\begin{Highlighting}[]

\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(patchwork)}
\NormalTok{p1 }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{ggscatter}\NormalTok{(miner[miner}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{time}\SpecialCharTok{==}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,],}
          \AttributeTok{x=}\StringTok{"age"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{y=}\StringTok{"vitalcp"}\NormalTok{,}
          \AttributeTok{color=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{add=}\StringTok{"reg.line"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{p2 }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{ggscatter}\NormalTok{(miner[miner}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{time}\SpecialCharTok{==}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,],}
          \AttributeTok{x=}\StringTok{"age"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{y=}\StringTok{"vitalcp"}\NormalTok{,}
          \AttributeTok{color=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{add=}\StringTok{"reg.line"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{p3 }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{ggscatter}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{data =} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{pred =} \FunctionTok{fitted}\NormalTok{(cfit),}
  \AttributeTok{resid =} \FunctionTok{residuals}\NormalTok{(cfit)}
\NormalTok{),}\AttributeTok{x=}\StringTok{"pred"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{y=}\StringTok{"resid"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{color=}\StringTok{"green"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{add=}\StringTok{"reg.line"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{(p1}\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{p2)}\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{p3}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-46-1}

\hypertarget{report-the-data}{%
\subsection{Report the Data}\label{report-the-data}}

ANCOVA was conducted on \texttt{vitalcp} with \texttt{time} as the main
effect and \texttt{age} as the covariate.

The covariate, age of the workers, was significantly related to the
vital capacity,
\(F(1,25)=19.775, p<0.001, r = 0.665,\eta^2p=0.43389844\). There was no
significant effect of time on the vital capacity, after controlling the
effect of age, \(F(1,25) = 0.965, p=0.33, \eta^2p= 0.03791233\).

\hypertarget{latin-square}{%
\section{Latin Square}\label{latin-square}}

The following data \texttt{yie} has the information about the yields of
vegetables, say \texttt{yield}, and the corresponding conditions of
growing, such as the variety of vegetables, say \texttt{variety} here;
and the location information for \texttt{rep} and \texttt{col}. In the
three treatments, varieties of vegetables are the primary one that we're
interested in, and the other two are secondary variables to be
controlled.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{knitr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{include\_graphics}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Latin Square.png\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{yie }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ readxl}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{read\_xlsx}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"veget.xlsx"}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} 
\NormalTok{  rstatix}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{convert\_as\_factor}\NormalTok{(rep,col,variety)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{fit-the-model-1}{%
\subsection{Fit the Model}\label{fit-the-model-1}}

The model is fitted the same as before for ANOVA. Note that Latin Square
is a special design, where each letter (level) appears only once in each
row and each column.

Here, though the factors differ in importance, the order that they're
plugged in does not matter!

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{latinfit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{aov}\NormalTok{(yield}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{rep}\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{col}\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{variety,}
           \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ yie)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{summary-the-model-1}{%
\subsection{Summary the Model}\label{summary-the-model-1}}

\hypertarget{anova-view}{%
\subsubsection{ANOVA View}\label{anova-view}}

Basically, \texttt{summary(model)} and \texttt{anova(model)} will return
the same result.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# summary(latinfit)}
\FunctionTok{anova}\NormalTok{(latinfit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Analysis of Variance Table
## 
## Response: yield
##           Df Sum Sq Mean Sq F value  Pr(>F)  
## rep        5  4.460  0.8919  0.6461 0.66553  
## col        5  1.695  0.3389  0.2455 0.94034  
## variety    5 21.563  4.3126  3.1242 0.01477 *
## Residuals 56 77.302  1.3804                  
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{linear-model-view-1}{%
\subsubsection{Linear-Model View}\label{linear-model-view-1}}

ANOVA (even Latin Square design) is a special case of linear model.
Similarly, use
\texttt{car::Anova(model,type\ =\ \textquotesingle{}III\textquotesingle{})}
to see the intercept term, with other terms same with the results from
the functions mentioned above.

However, if one grouping variable has multiple levels, the linear model
may seems not so simple, because each level will sit at a term. In this
case, traditional view of ANOVA seems ``better''.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{car}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{Anova}\NormalTok{(latinfit, }\AttributeTok{type =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}III\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Anova Table (Type III tests)
## 
## Response: yield
##              Sum Sq Df  F value  Pr(>F)    
## (Intercept) 1342.92  1 972.8543 < 2e-16 ***
## rep            4.46  5   0.6461 0.66553    
## col            1.69  5   0.2455 0.94034    
## variety       21.56  5   3.1242 0.01477 *  
## Residuals     77.30 56                     
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
