\hypertarget{nested-anova}{%
\section{Nested ANOVA}\label{nested-anova}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{nestedData }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ readxl}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{read\_excel}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"nested anova.xlsx"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{visualization}{%
\subsection{visualization}\label{visualization}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# we should eyeball the data first to make sense of the test.}
\CommentTok{\# create boxplots to visualize the data}
\FunctionTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(nestedData, }\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x=}\FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(location), }\AttributeTok{y=}\NormalTok{DV)) }\SpecialCharTok{+} 
  \FunctionTok{geom\_boxplot}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-54-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{nested-anova-1}{%
\subsection{nested ANOVA}\label{nested-anova-1}}

Theoretically, the order of computing the variance sum of squares
matters, thus appointing the
\texttt{summation\ =\ \textquotesingle{}I\textquotesingle{}}.

However, here the summation type does not matter.

Note that the result of the main effect of the group is wrong. We can
manually make it straight from the SS of the group and the subgroup.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# note that nested anova can use error type I and III.}
\CommentTok{\# aov() has a default type I to process factors in an sequential order}
\CommentTok{\# here type I and III don\textquotesingle{}t differ, since effectively we have no orders}
\NormalTok{aov\_model }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{aov}\NormalTok{(DV }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(method)}\SpecialCharTok{/}\FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(location), }
                \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ nestedData,}\AttributeTok{summation=}\StringTok{"I"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Warning: In lm.fit(x, y, offset = offset, singular.ok = singular.ok, ...) :
##  extra argument 'summation' will be disregarded
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(aov\_model)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                                 Df Sum Sq Mean Sq F value   Pr(>F)    
## factor(method)                   2  665.7   332.8   255.7 1.45e-10 ***
## factor(method):factor(location)  9 1720.7   191.2   146.9 6.98e-11 ***
## Residuals                       12   15.6     1.3                     
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{error-adjustment}{%
\subsubsection{error adjustment}\label{error-adjustment}}

An alternative refinement is to adjust the error term.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{aov.result2 }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{aov}\NormalTok{(DV }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(method) }\SpecialCharTok{+} 
                    \FunctionTok{Error}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(method)}\SpecialCharTok{:}\FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(location)),}
                  \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ nestedData)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Warning in aov(DV ~ factor(method) + Error(factor(method):factor(location)), :
## Error() model is singular
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(aov.result2)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Error: factor(method):factor(location)
##                Df Sum Sq Mean Sq F value Pr(>F)
## factor(method)  2  665.7   332.8   1.741   0.23
## Residuals       9 1720.7   191.2               
## 
## Error: Within
##           Df Sum Sq Mean Sq F value Pr(>F)
## Residuals 12  15.62   1.302
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{comparison-with-one-way}{%
\subsubsection{comparison with one-way}\label{comparison-with-one-way}}

If we wrongly ignore the nested factor and use one-way ANOVA, the
results would be different

Note that there's only one group factor, therefore, the type of
summation does not matter.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{aov\_model }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{aov}\NormalTok{(DV }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(method), }
                \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ nestedData,}\AttributeTok{summation=}\StringTok{"III"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Warning: In lm.fit(x, y, offset = offset, singular.ok = singular.ok, ...) :
##  extra argument 'summation' will be disregarded
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(aov\_model)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                Df Sum Sq Mean Sq F value Pr(>F)  
## factor(method)  2  665.7   332.8   4.026 0.0331 *
## Residuals      21 1736.3    82.7                 
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{aov\_model }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{aov}\NormalTok{(DV }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(method), }
                \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ nestedData)}

\hypertarget{classical-not-preferred}{%

Not preferred. It's not efficient to do this way.

The specific function \texttt{mshapiro.test()} requires to library the
package \texttt{mvnormtest}, which surprisingly has a single function!
Moreover, it requires the data to be transposed before being processed.

The most annoying part is that the data has to be packed purposefully.

\begin{Shaded}

\hypertarget{homogeniety-of-variance-covariance}{%
\subsubsection{Homogeniety of Variance \&
Covariance}\label{homogeniety-of-variance-covariance}}

Using the Box's M-test and Levene's Test.

\hypertarget{pipe-friendly-preferred-1}{%
\paragraph{pipe-friendly (Preferred)}\label{pipe-friendly-preferred-1}}

Using \texttt{box\_m()} from the package \texttt{rstatix} to test the
homogeniety of covariance matrices, in which the first parameter is the
DVs needed to be analyzed (No group variable! This can be achieved
through \texttt{select()}), and the second is the \texttt{group}
parameter specifying the group variable.

Moreover, the function \texttt{heplots::boxM()} is helpful. Instead of
taking the formula to specify the parameters, using the ``traditional''
way to write \texttt{group\ =\ ...} works pretty well combined with
pipe-friendly environment!

The drawback of the functions is that they \textbf{cannot search column
variables automatically}, and that should be specified
\textbf{manually}. And more annoying one is that, when encountering with
NAs, an error will be raised by \texttt{rstatix::box\_m()}, while that's
fine with \texttt{heplots::boxM()}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{salaryData }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(salary,salbegin) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}
\NormalTok{  rstatix}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{box\_m}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{group =}\NormalTok{ salaryData}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{jobcat)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 1 x 4
##   statistic   p.value parameter method                                          
##       <dbl>     <dbl>     <dbl> <chr>                                           
## 1      509. 1.14e-106         6 Box's M-test for Homogeneity of Covariance Matr~
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\end{verbatim}

Do not forget that the assumption of homogeniety of variance for each DV
is needed either! However, the traditional \texttt{levene\_test()} (or
its variants) can deal with one DV at a time, which is tedious when we
have many DVs to check.

Hopefully, we have \texttt{heplots::leveneTests(data,group)} to deal
with that issue. Combined with pipe-friendly procedures, that's
fabulous!

One noteworthy flaw is that the
\texttt{heplots::leveneTests(data,group)} cannot search column variables
like that in the ``tidyverse'' environment.

\hypertarget{logistic-regression}{%
\section{Logistic Regression}\label{logistic-regression}}

Logistic Regression is for fitting a regression \textbf{curve} to data
where the DV is \textbf{dichotomous}. Meanwhile, the IV(s) can take on
any form, such as binary, categorical and continuous.

The goal of logistic regression is to model the probability of a certain
event (or say, a specific input). However, probability \(p\) is not used
directly here. Use \textbf{Odds} instead.

\[
Odds=\frac{p}{1-p}
\]

The logistic function used as such a predictor is as follows.

\[
p(z)=\frac1{1+e^{-z}}=\frac{e^{z}}{1+e^{z}}
\]

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-73-1.pdf}

The logistic function performs well that it's the C.D.F of a normal
distribution! Or take derivative of the logistic function, beautiful and
unique traits of the normal distribution can be well applied.

In the logistic function, \(z=\alpha+\beta x\), which can also be
extended to multiple IVs, i.e.~\(z = \beta_0+\beta_1+...+\beta_n x\).

Obviously, if the coefficient of IV is positive, then the probability
will increase with IV. If not significantly different from zero, then
probability won't significantly be affected by it.

Take a step further, the coefficient \(\beta\) is interpreted through
the term ``\textbf{Odds Ratio}'', where

\[
Odds\ Ratio = \frac{adds(x_i+1)}{odds(x_i)}=e^{\beta_i}
\]

The odds ratio is the effect size of corresponding IV \(x_i\), which
means one unit increase in x would induce the odds to change to
\(e^{\beta_i}\) times of itself. The direction given by it coincides
with previous discussion of the sign of coefficients and direction of
odds' change. Then, if \(\beta_i\) is statistically differently from 0
(through \(z\) test or Wald test, equivalent), then the odds ratio is
significantly different from 1, and 1 doesn't fall in its CI.

Furthermore, given an observation, the CI of probability can be
obtained.

All above seems strange at first glance. However, the focus on odds
isn't odd. From the logistic function, doing some transformation, we can
get the logit function.

\[
Logit(p)=\ln(\frac{p}{1-p})=\alpha+\beta x
\]

The logit function is linear and the response variable has much to do
with odds!

However, the linear regression line here isn't obtained
``traditionally'' as we did. Maximum Likelihood Estimation (MLE) is
used, instead of Ordinary Least Squares (OLS).

The following picture can help understand why MLE.

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{Logit transformation}

For real data, the observed outcome can be either 0 or 1. However, that
won't make any sense considering linear transforming that into
\(Logit(p)=\ln(\frac{p}{1-p})\), either \(-\infty\) or \(+\infty\).
Thus, OLS doesn't work here.

Alternatively, MLE is used here to estimate a ``good'' fit. Given an
arbitrary regression line, each point is projected onto the line
according to its \textbf{\(x\)} value(s). Then the goodness of such fit
is rated by its ``likelihood'' with the reality, which means better
model should explain and predict the data better， thus depicting the
world better.

The likelihood used here is as follows.

\[
\begin{aligned}
\text{L}(data) & =\Pi_{i=1}^n [p(x_i)^{y_i}(1-p(x_i))^{1-y_i}]\\
\text{LL}(data)& =\sum_{i=1}^n [{y_i}\times p(x_i)+{1-y_i}\times(1-p(x_i))]
\end{aligned}
\]

Starting from a seemingly ``arbitrary'' line (the initial parameter
should be specified or guessed by human, and that matters when it comes
to efficiency.), the algorithm is smart enough that it'll change the
parameters repeatedly until for a direction with higher likelihood, and
ultimately find the maximized one and the corresponding parameters as
the solution.

Finally, the fitted result can be evaluated through four aspects.

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{evaluation of logitstic model}

\[
\begin{aligned}
Accuracy&=\frac{TP+TN}{TP+TN+FP+FN}\\
Precision&=\frac{TP}{TP+FP}\\
Sensitivity&=\frac{TP}{TP+FN}\\
Specificity&=\frac{TN}{TN+FP}
\end{aligned}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Accuracy is the rate that the model can predict true results,
  \textbf{either positive or negative}.
\item
  Precision is the rate that the model can predict true positive
  results, \textbf{within reported positive ones}.
\item
  Sensitivity is the rate that the model can predict true positive
  results, \textbf{within actual positive ones}.
\item
  Specificity is the rate that the model can predict the true
  \textbf{negative} results, \textbf{within actual negative ones}.
\end{itemize}

How many parameters the mdoel should have in order to have an effective
and efficient prediction? More parameters added into a certain model
will increase accuracy for the present data (sometimes even won't),
however, such complicated model may lose generalizability and lose its
ability to predict. Well, on the other hand, simple model may not have
high ability to explain or predict the data. We should find a
compromise.

\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{fitting model}

Generally speaking, for models evaluated by MLE, the comparison of MLEs
under different hypothesis can be applied to model comparison.

\[
\begin{aligned}
H_0&:\theta=\theta_0\\
H_1&:\theta=\theta_1\\
\Lambda(y)&=\frac{L(\theta_1|y)}{L(\theta_2|y)}
\end{aligned}\\
If\ \Lambda(y)>c,\ reject\ H_0
\]

Based on above, here to introduce the nested hypothesis test.
Apparently, the nested model is simpler, we're to check necessity of the
addition of other IV(s).

Suppose Model 0 (\(M_0\)) is nested in Model 1 (\(M_1\)). \(M_0\) has
\(m\) parameters, and \(M_1\) has \(n\). Define

\[
\Delta(y) = 2\ln(L(\hat{\theta_1}|y)-2\ln(L(\hat{\theta_0}|y))
\]

Here factor ``2'' is to scale the statistic to make it that

\[
\Delta(y)\sim \chi_{n-m}^2
\]

Hence, if \(\Delta(y)\) exceeds the critical value, reject \(M_0\) and
recognize that \(M_1\) is simpler but not worse (i.e.~general and
powerful).

Often, a model with intercept and predictors (B) is compared to an
intercept-only model to test whether the predictors contribute something
over and above the intercept-only.

Note that considering sample size when evaluating goodness of fit,
\(\chi^2\) statistics are heavily influenced by sample size so that with
a very large sample, even a tiny difference will be significant. If the
sample size is large and the \(\chi^2\) is significant, this may not be
important.

Coming back, there're three ways for model fitting.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Enter: all theoretically-based variables are added at once.
\item
  Forward Selection: one parameter at a time, significant ones kept.
  Used for exploratory purposes.
\item
  Backward Elimination: full model first, eliminate one by one. Used for
  finding a concise model.
\end{itemize}

All three way should be based on the significance of \(\chi^2\) test.

Meanwhile, there're more concepts or evaluations for model fitting,
indicating goodness of fit.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Nagelkerke \(r^2\): between 0 and 1, similar to \(r^2\), preferred.
\item
  Hosmer and Lemeshow (HL) test: compare the observed frequency and the
  predicted frequency, an alternative to model \(\chi^2\) test. If NOT
  significant, good!
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{fit-the-model-2}{%
\subsection{Fit the Model}\label{fit-the-model-2}}

Using the data \texttt{logisticData}, we're to predict whether or not to
have cancer through logistic regression model, with \texttt{age},
\texttt{pathsize} and \texttt{pathscat} (3 types) as predictors.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{logisticData }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ readxl}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{read\_xlsx}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Data11{-}02.xlsx"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Observations with \texttt{pathscat\ ==\ 99} are invalid and should be
eliminated.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{logisticData }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ logisticData }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} \FunctionTok{filter}\NormalTok{(pathscat }\SpecialCharTok{!=} \DecValTok{99}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Then, set dummy variables.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{logisticData }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{model.matrix}\NormalTok{(}
    \SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(pathscat),logisticData)) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} 
\NormalTok{  dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} 
  \FunctionTok{rename}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{pathscat2 =}\NormalTok{ factor.pathscat}\FloatTok{.2}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{pathscat3 =}\NormalTok{ factor.pathscat}\FloatTok{.3}
\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}
  \FunctionTok{cbind}\NormalTok{(logisticData)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Setting dummies is quite important when having categorical data, and for
more details, see appendix.

Use
\texttt{glm(formula,\ data,\ family\ =\ binomial(\textquotesingle{}logit\textquotesingle{}))}
to get the logistic model. Here \texttt{glm()} stands for ``Generalized
Linear Model''.

As for the model, others are the same with what we've learned.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{glm}\NormalTok{(ln\_yesno }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ age }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathsize }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathscat2 }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathscat3,}
          \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ logisticData,}
          \AttributeTok{family =} \FunctionTok{binomial}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}logit\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Note that \texttt{\textquotesingle{}logit\textquotesingle{}} is the
default for
\texttt{binomial(\textquotesingle{}logit\textquotesingle{}))}.

Not that in \texttt{lm()}, categorical variables can be thrown in
directly, without setting dummies tediously. But the categorical
variable should be ``factor'' or ``character'', not ``numeric''.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Call:
## glm(formula = ln_yesno ~ age + pathsize + pathscat2 + pathscat3, 
##     family = binomial("logit"), data = logisticData)
## 
## Deviance Residuals: 
##     Min       1Q   Median       3Q      Max  
## -1.9676  -0.7400  -0.6134  -0.4497   2.1834  
## 
## Coefficients:
##              Estimate Std. Error z value Pr(>|z|)    
## (Intercept) -0.583306   0.391314  -1.491   0.1361    
## age         -0.024646   0.005764  -4.276  1.9e-05 ***
## pathsize     0.423857   0.130885   3.238   0.0012 ** 
## pathscat2   -0.122002   0.266498  -0.458   0.6471    
## pathscat3    0.184969   0.845896   0.219   0.8269    
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
## 
## (Dispersion parameter for binomial family taken to be 1)
## 
##     Null deviance: 1216.7  on 1120  degrees of freedom
## Residual deviance: 1151.8  on 1116  degrees of freedom
## AIC: 1161.8
## 
## Number of Fisher Scoring iterations: 4
\end{verbatim}

The logistic regression model is different from the traditional OLS
model; there's no F value and multiple R squared. Instead, deviance of
the designated model and the null model are reported at bottom, no test
represented here. In fact, the \textbf{deviance} is 2 times the
\textbf{Log-Likelihood} taking the absolute value. and the larger the
AIC, the prettier the model comparison.

The effect size for each \(x_i\) is \(e^{\beta_i}\), which can be
obtained through a matrix. Interestingly, the \texttt{exp()} can be
exerted to a data frame and all values within.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{exp}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{cbind}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{OR =} \FunctionTok{coef}\NormalTok{(fit), }\FunctionTok{confint}\NormalTok{(fit)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Waiting for profiling to be done...
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
##                    OR     2.5 %    97.5 %
## (Intercept) 0.5580501 0.2582678 1.1992024
## age         0.9756550 0.9645972 0.9866601
## pathsize    1.5278427 1.1832781 1.9780602
## pathscat2   0.8851466 0.5234879 1.4893971
## pathscat3   1.2031815 0.2348608 6.7095766
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{goodness-of-fit-1}{%
\subsection{Goodness of Fit}\label{goodness-of-fit-1}}

\hypertarget{each-predictor}{%
\subsubsection{Each Predictor}\label{each-predictor}}

\hypertarget{separately}{%
\paragraph{Separately}\label{separately}}

Use \texttt{aod::wald.test(Sigma,\ b,\ Terms)} to carry the Wald test
for each coefficient.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{Sigma}: a var-cov matrix of the model, extracted from
  \texttt{vcov(model)}.
\item
  \texttt{b}: a vector of coefficients with var-cov matrix
  \texttt{Sigma}, extracted from \texttt{coef(model)}.
\item
  \texttt{Terms}: an optional integer vector specifying which
  coefficients should be jointly tested, like choosing the variable from
  the var-cov matrix.
\end{itemize}

The result of significance of the Wald test is the same as is reported
in \texttt{summary(model)}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{aod}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{wald.test}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{Sigma =} \FunctionTok{vcov}\NormalTok{(fit),}
  \AttributeTok{b =} \FunctionTok{coef}\NormalTok{(fit), }
  \AttributeTok{Terms =} \DecValTok{2}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Wald test:
## ----------
## 
## Chi-squared test:
## X2 = 18.3, df = 1, P(> X2) = 1.9e-05
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{forward-selection}{%
\paragraph{Forward Selection}\label{forward-selection}}

Each predictor is tested sequentially, corresponding to ``forward
selection''.

Note that if you didn't set dummies encountering with categorical
variables, fine with the linear model, but here goes wrong!

Note that \texttt{test\ =\ \textquotesingle{}Chisq\textquotesingle{}}
with capital `C', otherwise an error will be raised.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{anova}\NormalTok{(fit,}\AttributeTok{test =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Chisq\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Analysis of Deviance Table
## 
## Model: binomial, link: logit
## 
## Response: ln_yesno
## 
## Terms added sequentially (first to last)
## 
## 
##           Df Deviance Resid. Df Resid. Dev  Pr(>Chi)    
## NULL                       1120     1216.7              
## age        1   32.315      1119     1184.3 1.311e-08 ***
## pathsize   1   32.024      1118     1152.3 1.523e-08 ***
## pathscat2  1    0.510      1117     1151.8    0.4750    
## pathscat3  1    0.048      1116     1151.8    0.8265    
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{overall-model}{%
\subsubsection{Overall Model}\label{overall-model}}

The deviance is the absolute value of 2 times the log-likelihood, the
difference of the deviance of the null model and the designated model is
the statistic used for the model comparison, which follows a \(\chi^2\)
distribution with its degree of freedom as the difference of the number
of parameter in two models.

\[
\Delta(y)=deviance_{null}-deviance\sim \chi^2_n,n=\#IV
\]

Therefore, we can even carry the model test manually.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{with}\NormalTok{(fit, null.deviance }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ deviance)}
\FunctionTok{with}\NormalTok{(fit, df.null }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ df.residual)}
\FunctionTok{with}\NormalTok{(fit, }\FunctionTok{pchisq}\NormalTok{(null.deviance }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ deviance, }
\NormalTok{                 df.null }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ df.residual, }
                 \AttributeTok{lower.tail =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The result of model comparison is highly significant, meaning that the
designated model can explain far more variation of the data than the
null one.

But better to use
\texttt{anova(model1,null-model,test\ =\ \textquotesingle{}Chisq\textquotesingle{})}.
Same result.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{nullfit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{glm}\NormalTok{(ln\_yesno }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}
          \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ logisticData,}
          \AttributeTok{family =} \FunctionTok{binomial}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}logit\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\FunctionTok{anova}\NormalTok{(fit,nullfit,}\AttributeTok{test =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Chisq\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Analysis of Deviance Table
## 
## Model 1: ln_yesno ~ age + pathsize + pathscat2 + pathscat3
## Model 2: ln_yesno ~ 1
##   Resid. Df Resid. Dev Df Deviance  Pr(>Chi)    
## 1      1116     1151.8                          
## 2      1120     1216.7 -4  -64.897 2.705e-13 ***
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{model-fitting}{%
\subsubsection{Model Fitting}\label{model-fitting}}

The designated model wins, but that unnecessarily means it can explains
the data well. Then we're to take the test of goodness of fit, checking
the observed and predicted frequencies. No significance is wanted here.

Methods for such model fitting varies. Here we use HL-test. Use
\texttt{ResourceSelection::hoslem.test(x\ =\ observed\_values,y\ =\ expected\_values,g\ =\ 10)},
where \texttt{g} is the number of bins to calculate quantiles and
\texttt{x} \& \texttt{y} cannot be reversed.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ResourceSelection}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{hoslem.test}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ logisticData}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{ln\_yesno, }
                               \AttributeTok{y =} \FunctionTok{fitted}\NormalTok{(fit), }
                               \AttributeTok{g =} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Hosmer and Lemeshow goodness of fit (GOF) test
## 
## data:  logisticData$ln_yesno, fitted(fit)
## X-squared = 8.9727, df = 8, p-value = 0.3446
\end{verbatim}

\emph{Note that the bins set should not be so dense that the sample size
within each bin will fall short.}

\hypertarget{fitting-quality}{%
\subsubsection{Fitting Quality}\label{fitting-quality}}

\hypertarget{log-likelihood}{%
\paragraph{Log-Likelihood}\label{log-likelihood}}

Log-likelihood can be obtained using \texttt{logLik(model)} directly,
with \texttt{df} telling parameters in the model (including the
intercept).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{logLik}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 'log Lik.' -575.8852 (df=5)
\end{verbatim}

Log-likelihood can be obtained through \emph{deviance}, which is 2 times
the log-likelihood, and you can get that using \texttt{model\$deviance}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{deviance}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 1151.77
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{r-squares}{%
\paragraph{R Squares}\label{r-squares}}

Obtain all 5 R Squares by \texttt{pscl::pR2(model)}.

Hint:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{r2CU} is the Nagelkerke R2;
\item
  \texttt{r2ML} is the Cox\&Snell R2;
\item
  \texttt{llh} is the log-likelihood of the designated model;
\item
  \texttt{llhNull} is the log-likelihood of the null model.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{pscl}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{pR2}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## fitting null model for pseudo-r2
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
##           llh       llhNull            G2      McFadden          r2ML 
## -575.88522800 -608.33388920   64.89732239    0.05334022    0.05624846 
##          r2CU 
##    0.08494006
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{model-comparison}{%
\subsubsection{Model Comparison}\label{model-comparison}}

You can compare one model against another using
\texttt{anova(model1,model2,test\ =\ \textquotesingle{}Chisq\textquotesingle{})}.

For example, it's natural to think of an alternative model from what
we've fitted originally. Just keep all significant IV(s), and we get a
new model to be compared against.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit2 }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{glm}\NormalTok{(ln\_yesno }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ age }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathsize,}
           \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ logisticData,}
           \AttributeTok{family =} \FunctionTok{binomial}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"logit"}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{anova}\NormalTok{(fit,fit2,}\AttributeTok{test =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Chisq\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Analysis of Deviance Table
## 
## Model 1: ln_yesno ~ age + pathsize + pathscat2 + pathscat3
## Model 2: ln_yesno ~ age + pathsize
##   Resid. Df Resid. Dev Df Deviance Pr(>Chi)
## 1      1116     1151.8                     
## 2      1118     1152.3 -2 -0.55846   0.7564
\end{verbatim}

The significance is shown under \texttt{Pr(\textgreater{}Chi)}. And
\texttt{Df} shows the degree of freedom of the certain \(\chi^2\)
distribution. \texttt{Deviance} shows the \(\chi^2\) statistic value.

In this case, a simpler model does not perform statistically worse and
is considered better.

\hypertarget{classification-table}{%
\subsubsection{Classification Table}\label{classification-table}}

It's the last step of logistic regression. Just see how well the model
works when putting that into practice!

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{pred\_y }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{round}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{predict}\NormalTok{(fit,}\AttributeTok{type=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}response\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{),}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{classification\_df }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{observed\_y =}\NormalTok{ logisticData}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{ln\_yesno,}
  \AttributeTok{predicted\_y =} \FunctionTok{round}\NormalTok{(pred\_y,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{))}

\FunctionTok{xtabs}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{predicted\_y}\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{observed\_y,}\AttributeTok{data=}\NormalTok{classification\_df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##            observed_y
## predicted_y   0   1
##           0 846 246
##           1  14  15
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{report-results}{%
\subsection{Report Results}\label{report-results}}

A logistic regression analysis was conducted to predict the occurrence
of cancer using cancer category, age and tumor size as predictors. A
test of the full model against a constant-only model was statistically
significant, indicating that the predictors as a set reliably
distinguished between cancer and non-cancer
(\(χ^2(4)= 64.897, p < .001\)).

Nagelkerke's R2 of \(.085\) indicated a weak relationship between
prediction and grouping. Prediction success overall was 76.8\% (98.4\%
for detecting a non-cancer and 5.7\% for cancer). The Wald criterion
demonstrated that only age and tumor size made a significant
contribution to prediction (p \textless{} .001 and p =.001,
respectively). Cancer category was not a significant predictor. EXP(B)
value indicates that when tumor size is raised by one unit the odds
ratio is 1.5 times as large and therefore the tumor are 1.5 more times
likely to be cancer.

\hypertarget{multi-linear-regression}{%
\section{Multi-Linear Regression}\label{multi-linear-regression}}

The data \texttt{df} presented in MLR contains information about salary,
beginning salary, education year, job category, previous working
experience and so on. The relationship between salary and beginning
salary, education, to name a few, is of interest.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{df }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ readxl}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{read\_excel}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"data02{-}01.xlsx"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{sheet =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{attach}\NormalTok{(df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## The following objects are masked _by_ .GlobalEnv:
## 
##     age, jobcat
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{pre-inspection-1}{%
\subsection{Pre-Inspection}\label{pre-inspection-1}}

\hypertarget{linear-relationship}{%
\subsubsection{linear relationship}\label{linear-relationship}}

Use \texttt{cor()} to carry the correlation test, the default method of
which is
\texttt{method\ =\ \textquotesingle{}pearson\textquotesingle{}}. The
result will be presented as a correlation matrix.

However, obviously the correlation matrix won't give you information
about p-value (or say, significance).

Good thing is that the check of linear relationship is the first and
rough step for further regression, maybe other more information (about
p-value or CIs) is unnecessary. Another good thing is that the
\texttt{cor()} is from base R, no extra requirement to library packages.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{df }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(salary,educ,salbegin,jobtime,prevexp) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} \FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##               salary        educ    salbegin      jobtime      prevexp
## salary    1.00000000  0.66055891  0.88011747  0.084092267 -0.097466926
## educ      0.66055891  1.00000000  0.63319565  0.047378777 -0.252352521
## salbegin  0.88011747  0.63319565  1.00000000 -0.019753475  0.045135627
## jobtime   0.08409227  0.04737878 -0.01975347  1.000000000  0.002978134
## prevexp  -0.09746693 -0.25235252  0.04513563  0.002978134  1.000000000
\end{verbatim}

Well, it's clumsy to compress the target data (variables) into a matrix
to take the test and get the correlation matrix. Just fine to know a bit
about that.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{attach}\NormalTok{(df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## The following objects are masked _by_ .GlobalEnv:
## 
##     age, jobcat
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
## The following objects are masked from df (pos = 3):
## 
##     age, bdate, educ, gender, id, jobcat, jobtime, minority, prevexp,
##     salary, salbegin
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# first create an empty matrix, primarily filled with NA}
\NormalTok{m }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{matrix}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{nrow=}\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(salaryData),}\AttributeTok{ncol=}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{m[,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{salary}
\NormalTok{m[,}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{]}\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{educ}
\NormalTok{m[,}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{] }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ salbegin}
\NormalTok{m[,}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{]}\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{jobtime  }
\NormalTok{m[,}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{]}\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{prevexp}
\CommentTok{\#check the correlation matrix}
\FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{(m)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##             [,1]        [,2]        [,3]         [,4]         [,5]
## [1,]  1.00000000  0.66055891  0.88011747  0.084092267 -0.097466926
## [2,]  0.66055891  1.00000000  0.63319565  0.047378777 -0.252352521
## [3,]  0.88011747  0.63319565  1.00000000 -0.019753475  0.045135627
## [4,]  0.08409227  0.04737878 -0.01975347  1.000000000  0.002978134
## [5,] -0.09746693 -0.25235252  0.04513563  0.002978134  1.000000000
\end{verbatim}

Additionally, I'd like to use \texttt{bruceR::Corr()} for the
correlation matrix, which is visible for both in amount and in
significance. Moreover, it's fancy!

For simplicity, the two decimals by default is well enough. If more
accuracy is needed, you can set the parameter \texttt{digits\ =}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{df }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(salary,educ,salbegin,jobtime,prevexp) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} 
\NormalTok{  bruceR}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{Corr}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{digits =} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-98-1.pdf}

\begin{verbatim}
## Correlation matrix is displayed in the RStudio `Plots` Pane.
## 
## Pearson's r and 95% confidence intervals:
## ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
##                       r       [95% CI]     p       N
## ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
## salary-educ        0.66 [ 0.61,  0.71] <.001 *** 474
## salary-salbegin    0.88 [ 0.86,  0.90] <.001 *** 474
## salary-jobtime     0.08 [-0.01,  0.17]  .067 .   474
## salary-prevexp    -0.10 [-0.19, -0.01]  .034 *   474
## educ-salbegin      0.63 [ 0.58,  0.68] <.001 *** 474
## educ-jobtime       0.05 [-0.04,  0.14]  .303     474
## educ-prevexp      -0.25 [-0.33, -0.17] <.001 *** 474
## salbegin-jobtime  -0.02 [-0.11,  0.07]  .668     474
## salbegin-prevexp   0.05 [-0.05,  0.13]  .327     474
## jobtime-prevexp    0.00 [-0.09,  0.09]  .948     474
## ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{multicollinearity}{%
\subsubsection{multicollinearity}\label{multicollinearity}}

Use \texttt{car::vif(model)} and get the result directly.

For the convenience of narration, check of multicollinearity is
presented here. Most of the time, the check is a ``post-hoc'' one, after
obtaining the fitted model.

VIF less than 2 is ideal, and less than 5 is the bottom line.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{lm}\NormalTok{(salary }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ educ }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ salbegin }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ jobtime }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ prevexp,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ df)}
\NormalTok{car}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{vif}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##     educ salbegin  jobtime  prevexp 
## 1.937010 1.813582 1.007613 1.155814
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{fit-the-model-3}{%
\subsection{Fit the Model}\label{fit-the-model-3}}

\hypertarget{basic-summary}{%
\subsubsection{basic summary}\label{basic-summary}}

Firstly, use \texttt{lm()} to get the linear model.

Subsequently, use \texttt{summary()} to get the detailed result.

Period.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{lm}\NormalTok{(salary }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ educ }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ salbegin }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ jobtime }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ prevexp,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ df)}
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Call:
## lm(formula = salary ~ educ + salbegin + jobtime + prevexp, data = df)
## 
## Residuals:
##    Min     1Q Median     3Q    Max 
## -29600  -4119  -1246   2642  46079 
## 
## Coefficients:
##               Estimate Std. Error t value Pr(>|t|)    
## (Intercept) -1.615e+04  3.255e+03  -4.961 9.84e-07 ***
## educ         6.699e+02  1.656e+02   4.045 6.11e-05 ***
## salbegin     1.768e+00  5.873e-02  30.111  < 2e-16 ***
## jobtime      1.615e+02  3.425e+01   4.715 3.19e-06 ***
## prevexp     -1.730e+01  3.528e+00  -4.904 1.30e-06 ***
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
## 
## Residual standard error: 7465 on 469 degrees of freedom
## Multiple R-squared:  0.8105, Adjusted R-squared:  0.8089 
## F-statistic: 501.5 on 4 and 469 DF,  p-value: < 2.2e-16
\end{verbatim}

It's noteworthy that \texttt{bruceR::print\_table(model)} can spring up
the regression result as a decent table, and can be exported to a
\texttt{.doc} file.

However, fine with \texttt{summary()}, decency doesn't matter much here,
and the \texttt{summary()} can give more information about the model
itself that is essential, such F value and multiple R squared.

\hypertarget{coefficients}{%
\subsubsection{coefficients}\label{coefficients}}

Use \texttt{coefficients(model)} from base R to see the coefficients for
each IV.

Furthermore, use \texttt{confint(model,level\ =\ 0.95)} from base R to
see the CIs (95\% by default) for each coefficients.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{coefficients}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##   (Intercept)          educ      salbegin       jobtime       prevexp 
## -16149.671204    669.913570      1.768427    161.485642    -17.303251
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{confint}\NormalTok{(fit, }\AttributeTok{level=}\FloatTok{0.95}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                     2.5 %       97.5 %
## (Intercept) -22546.784635 -9752.557773
## educ           344.511104   995.316035
## salbegin         1.653019     1.883835
## jobtime         94.190179   228.781104
## prevexp        -24.236663   -10.369839
\end{verbatim}

Multiple R squared is reported through \texttt{summary()}.

By definition, Multiple R squared is defined as the square of the
correlation between estimated (fitted) value and real values.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{(salary,fit}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{fitted.values)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.9002721
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{model-comparison-1}{%
\subsubsection{model comparison}\label{model-comparison-1}}

Compare the model with the ``zero'' model, formally called the null
model. The null model has no IV but with intercept, which is the mean.
The null model uses the mean to predict the data or explain the
variation of values.

Get the null model by formula \texttt{DV\textasciitilde{}1}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit2 }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{lm}\NormalTok{(salary }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{ )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Then use \texttt{anova(model1,model2)} to compare the two models.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{anova}\NormalTok{(fit,fit2)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Analysis of Variance Table
## 
## Model 1: salary ~ educ + salbegin + jobtime + prevexp
## Model 2: salary ~ 1
##   Res.Df        RSS Df   Sum of Sq      F    Pr(>F)    
## 1    469 2.6137e+10                                    
## 2    473 1.3792e+11 -4 -1.1178e+11 501.45 < 2.2e-16 ***
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

If we think further, the F test for the MLR model is to test with null
hypothesis that all the coefficients of all the IVs are zero. Therefore,
the model comparison by essence coincides with the F test! Obviously,
the F value ``copies'' that in the \texttt{summary()} result.

\hypertarget{standard-mlr}{%
\subsection{Standard MLR}\label{standard-mlr}}

Transform the formula with each variable embedded into the function
\texttt{scale()}, no other adjustments!

Under standard MLR, the coefficients are standardized ones.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit\_std }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{lm}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{scale}\NormalTok{(salary) }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \FunctionTok{scale}\NormalTok{(educ) }\SpecialCharTok{+} \FunctionTok{scale}\NormalTok{(salbegin) }\SpecialCharTok{+} 
               \FunctionTok{scale}\NormalTok{(jobtime) }\SpecialCharTok{+} \FunctionTok{scale}\NormalTok{(prevexp),}
             \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(fit\_std)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Call:
## lm(formula = scale(salary) ~ scale(educ) + scale(salbegin) + 
##     scale(jobtime) + scale(prevexp), data = df)
## 
## Residuals:
##      Min       1Q   Median       3Q      Max 
## -1.73349 -0.24121 -0.07298  0.15475  2.69850 
## 
## Coefficients:
##                   Estimate Std. Error t value Pr(>|t|)    
## (Intercept)     -1.129e-16  2.008e-02   0.000        1    
## scale(educ)      1.132e-01  2.798e-02   4.045 6.11e-05 ***
## scale(salbegin)  8.151e-01  2.707e-02  30.111  < 2e-16 ***
## scale(jobtime)   9.515e-02  2.018e-02   4.715 3.19e-06 ***
## scale(prevexp)  -1.060e-01  2.161e-02  -4.904 1.30e-06 ***
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
## 
## Residual standard error: 0.4372 on 469 degrees of freedom
## Multiple R-squared:  0.8105, Adjusted R-squared:  0.8089 
## F-statistic: 501.5 on 4 and 469 DF,  p-value: < 2.2e-16
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{semi--partial-for-x}{%
\subsubsection{(semi-) partial for x}\label{semi--partial-for-x}}

(Semi-) partial correlation indicates the variance accounted for by the
certain IV specifically, which is especially intriguing and important in
standard MlR.

Use \texttt{pcor()} from the package \texttt{ppcor} to compute the
partial correlation for IVs.

Use \texttt{spcor()} from the package \texttt{ppcor} to compute the
semi-partial correlation for IVs.

Throw the regressed variables (both IVs \& DV) into the functions above,
and period.

The results will include estimated value of correlation and its p-value.
However, the drawback is that the returned matrix does not include
rownames and colnames, making it reading-unfriendly. However, we only
care about the (semi-) partial correlation between DV and all IVs, which
means only the very one row or column is enough.

Therefore, DV goes first! Only check the first row or column!

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# partial correlation}
\NormalTok{df }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(salary,educ,salbegin,jobtime,prevexp) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} 
\NormalTok{  ppcor}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{pcor}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## $estimate
##              salary        educ   salbegin     jobtime     prevexp
## salary    1.0000000  0.18362578  0.8118323  0.21275206 -0.22085427
## educ      0.1836258  1.00000000  0.2347785  0.04216897 -0.30912243
## salbegin  0.8118323  0.23477855  1.0000000 -0.21340370  0.33602452
## jobtime   0.2127521  0.04216897 -0.2134037  1.00000000  0.07981028
## prevexp  -0.2208543 -0.30912243  0.3360245  0.07981028  1.00000000

## $p.value
##                 salary         educ      salbegin      jobtime      prevexp

## salbegin 1.163309e-111 2.550384e-07  0.000000e+00 2.967847e-06 6.775217e-14

\hypertarget{goodness-of-fit-2}{%
\subsection{Goodness of Fit}\label{goodness-of-fit-2}}

\hypertarget{fitted-values-real-values}{%
\subsubsection{fitted values \& real
values}\label{fitted-values-real-values}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fitted\_y }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{fitted}\NormalTok{(fit) }\CommentTok{\# predicted values}
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(df}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{salary,fitted\_y,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-108-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{residual}{%
\subsubsection{residual}\label{residual}}

If the model fits well, then the residual should follow a normal
distribution.

Fitted model's residuals can be obtained through
\texttt{residuals(model)} or \texttt{model\$residuals}. This works the
same for other attributes of a linear model, such as coefficients.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# hist(fit$residuals)}
\FunctionTok{hist}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{residuals}\NormalTok{(fit))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-109-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{plot-more}{%
\subsubsection{plot more}\label{plot-more}}

Use \texttt{plot(model)} to get multiple plots reflecting the goodness
of fit from many perspectives.

Specify parameter \texttt{which\ =\ vector\_of\_range} to designate the
plots to show, 6 the most. \texttt{which\ =\ 1:4} by default.

Following the order, the six plots are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Residuals \& Fitted Values

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    test the homoscedascitiy assumption
  \end{itemize}
\item
  QQ Plot for Residuals

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    test the Gaussian assumption
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Square Root of Standardized Residuals \& Fitted Value

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    similar to the first one
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Cook's Distance

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    check outliers
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Standardized Residuals \& Leverage

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    See if influential points have huge residual, if so, possibly
    outlier!
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Cook's Distance \& Leverage

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Large in both, possibly outlier!
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(fit, }\AttributeTok{which=}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-110-1.pdf}
\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-110-2.pdf}
\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-110-3.pdf}
\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-110-4.pdf}
\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-110-5.pdf}
\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-110-6.pdf}

There's another way to do the model checking. Use
\texttt{performance::check\_model(model)}.

The default option is to check all the ``vif'', ``qq'', ``normality'',
``linearity'', ``ncv'', ``homogeneity'', ``outliers'', ``reqq'',
``pp\_check'', ``binned\_residuals'' and ``overdispersion'', which can
be specified through \texttt{check\ =\ specifying\_vector}. Here I'll
take the check of outliers as an example.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{performance}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{check\_model}\NormalTok{(fit,}\AttributeTok{check =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}outliers\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-111-1.pdf}

Do not forget to \texttt{detach()} the attached data.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{detach}\NormalTok{(df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{appendix}{%
\section{Appendix}\label{appendix}}

\hypertarget{brucermanova}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{bruceR::MANOVA()}}{bruceR::MANOVA()}}\label{brucermanova}}

\texttt{bruceR::MANOVA()} is an old friend. What's new here is that, for
covariate(s), just specify the parameter \texttt{covariate}. If single,
a character is good; if multiple, compress charactors into a factor.

Mind the differences between \texttt{covariate} and \texttt{between}.
The latter should put interaction(s) into consideration.

For the ANCOVA example above,

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ancfit }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ bruceR}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{MANOVA}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ miner, }
                        \AttributeTok{dv =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}vitalcp\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
                        \AttributeTok{between =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}time\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
                        \AttributeTok{covariate =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Warning: Numerical variables NOT centered on 0 (matters if variable in interaction):
##    age
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## ====== ANOVA (Between-Subjects Design) ======
## 
## Descriptives:
## ────────────────────────
##  "time"  Mean    S.D.  n
## ────────────────────────
##   time1 3.949 (1.198) 12
##   time2 4.122 (0.768) 16
## ────────────────────────
## Total sample size: N = 28
## 
## ANOVA Table:
## Dependent variable(s):      vitalcp
## Between-subjects factor(s): time
## Within-subjects factor(s):  –
## Covariate(s):               age
## ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
##           MS   MSE df1 df2      F     p     η²p [90% CI of η²p]  η²G
## ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
## time   0.542 0.550   1  25  0.985  .330       .038 [.000, .218] .038
## age   10.881 0.550   1  25 19.775 <.001 ***   .442 [.196, .617] .442
## ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
## MSE = mean square error (the residual variance of the linear model)
## η²p = partial eta-squared = SS / (SS + SSE) = F * df1 / (F * df1 + df2)
## ω²p = partial omega-squared = (F - 1) * df1 / (F * df1 + df2 + 1)
## η²G = generalized eta-squared (see Olejnik & Algina, 2003)
## Cohen’s f² = η²p / (1 - η²p)
## 
## Levene’s Test for Homogeneity of Variance:
## ─────────────────────────────────────────
##              Levene’s F df1 df2     p    
## ─────────────────────────────────────────
## DV: vitalcp       2.701   1  26  .112    
## ─────────────────────────────────────────
\end{verbatim}

For the Latin Square design's example above,

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{latfit }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ bruceR}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{MANOVA}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ yie, }
                        \AttributeTok{dv =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
                        \AttributeTok{covariate =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}rep\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}col\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{),}
                        \AttributeTok{between =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}variety\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}

## ====== ANOVA (Between-Subjects Design) ======
## 
## Descriptives:
## ────────────────────────────
##  "variety"   Mean    S.D.  n
## ────────────────────────────
##   variety1 17.367 (1.026) 12

## Within-subjects factor(s):  –

## ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
## variety  4.313 1.380   5  56 3.124  .015 *     .218 [.029, .331] .218
## rep      0.892 1.380   5  56 0.646  .666       .055 [.000, .100] .055
## col      0.339 1.380   5  56 0.246  .940       .021 [.000, .008] .021

## MSE = mean square error (the residual variance of the linear model)

## ω²p = partial omega-squared = (F - 1) * df1 / (F * df1 + df2 + 1)
## η²G = generalized eta-squared (see Olejnik & Algina, 2003)
## Cohen’s f² = η²p / (1 - η²p)
## 
## Levene’s Test for Homogeneity of Variance:
## ───────────────────────────────────────
##            Levene’s F df1 df2     p    
## ───────────────────────────────────────

## ───────────────────────────────────────
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{set-dummies}{%
\subsection{Set Dummies}\label{set-dummies}}

\hypertarget{model.matrix}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{model.matrix()}}{model.matrix()}}\label{model.matrix}}

To set dummies in R, you can use the ``factor'' function to convert
categorical variables into a series of binary variables (i.e., dummies)
that can be used in a regression model.

Here's an example of how to set dummies in R:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# load the mtcars dataset}
\FunctionTok{data}\NormalTok{(mtcars)}

\CommentTok{\# convert the gear variable to a factor variable}
\NormalTok{mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{gear }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{gear)}

\CommentTok{\# create dummies for the gear variable}
\NormalTok{dummies }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{model.matrix}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{gear }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ mtcars)}

\CommentTok{\# view the dummies}
\FunctionTok{head}\NormalTok{(dummies)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                   gear3 gear4 gear5
## Mazda RX4             0     1     0
## Mazda RX4 Wag         0     1     0
## Datsun 710            0     1     0
## Hornet 4 Drive        1     0     0
## Hornet Sportabout     1     0     0
## Valiant               1     0     0
\end{verbatim}

In this example, we first load the mtcars dataset and convert the
\texttt{gear} variable to a factor variable using the \texttt{factor()}
function. This converts the \texttt{gear} variable \textbf{from a
numeric variable to a categorical variable, which can then be converted
to dummies}.

Next, we use the \texttt{model.matrix()} function to create dummies for
the \texttt{gear} variable. The ``\textasciitilde{}\texttt{gear} - 1''
formula tells R to create dummies for the \texttt{gear} variable, but to
\textbf{exclude the intercept term} (i.e., the constant term).

Finally, we view the dummies using the \texttt{head()} function. The
output shows a matrix with three columns, corresponding to the three
possible values of the \texttt{gear} variable (3, 4, and 5), and a row
for each observation in the mtcars dataset. Each element in the matrix
is either 0 or 1, representing whether or not the corresponding
observation has a particular value of the \texttt{gear} variable.

Note that dummies can also be created for multiple categorical variables
simultaneously using the ``cbind'' function. For example, if you have
two categorical variables ``\texttt{gear}'' and ``cyl'', you can create
dummies for both variables using the following code:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{dummies }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{cbind}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{model.matrix}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{gear }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ mtcars), }
                 \FunctionTok{model.matrix}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{cyl }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ mtcars))}

\FunctionTok{head}\NormalTok{(dummies)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                   gear3 gear4 gear5 cyl
## Mazda RX4             0     1     0   6
## Mazda RX4 Wag         0     1     0   6
## Datsun 710            0     1     0   4
## Hornet 4 Drive        1     0     0   6
## Hornet Sportabout     1     0     0   8
## Valiant               1     0     0   6
\end{verbatim}

This will create a matrix with columns for each possible value of the
\texttt{gear} variable and each possible value of the \texttt{cyl}
variable, and a row for each observation in the mtcars dataset.

Note that for a categorical variable with \(n\) levels, you can only
plug in \(n-1\) of the dummies into the regression data, in case of
multicollinearity.

\hypertarget{fastdummiesdummy_cols}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{fastDummies::dummy\_cols()}}{fastDummies::dummy\_cols()}}\label{fastdummiesdummy_cols}}

Pipe-friendly functions used to create dummies for categorical variables
include

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{fastDummies::dummy\_cols()}
\item
  \texttt{caret::dummyVars()}
\end{itemize}

Here's an example of how to use the \texttt{dummy\_cols()} function to
create dummies for the \texttt{gear} and \texttt{cyl} variable in the
\texttt{mtcars} dataset:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{mtcars }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
  \FunctionTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{gear =} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(gear),}
         \AttributeTok{cyl =} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(cyl)) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
\NormalTok{  fastDummies}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{dummy\_cols}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{select\_columns =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"gear"}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}cyl\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
    \AttributeTok{remove\_first\_dummy =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}
  \FunctionTok{head}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}}
\NormalTok{  dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{contains}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}gear\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{),}\FunctionTok{contains}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}cyl\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##   gear gear_4 gear_5 cyl cyl_6 cyl_8
## 1    4      1      0   6     1     0
## 2    4      1      0   6     1     0
## 3    4      1      0   4     0     0
## 4    3      0      0   6     1     0
## 5    3      0      0   8     0     1
## 6    3      0      0   6     1     0
\end{verbatim}

In this example, we use the \texttt{mutate} function to convert the
\texttt{gear} and \texttt{cyl} variable to a factor variable (it works
fine with \texttt{dummy\_cols()} even skip this step, but wrong with
\texttt{dummyVars()}). We then use the \texttt{dummy\_cols()} function
to create dummies for the \texttt{gear} and \texttt{cyl} variable. The
``select\_columns'' parameter specifies the column(s) to create dummies
for (packed in a vector), and the \texttt{remove\_first\_dummy}
parameter specifies whether or not to remove the first dummy (i.e., the
constant term).

The output of the \texttt{dummy\_cols()} function is a new data frame
with the dummies added as new columns. This can be piped into other
functions for further analysis or modeling.

\hypertarget{caretdummyvars}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{caret::dummyVars()}}{caret::dummyVars()}}\label{caretdummyvars}}

Usage of this function will be clear after a vivid example.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(caret)}
\FunctionTok{dummyVars}\NormalTok{( }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ gear }\SpecialCharTok{+} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(cyl), }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ mtcars) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} 
  \FunctionTok{predict}\NormalTok{(mtcars) }\SpecialCharTok{|\textgreater{}} \FunctionTok{head}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                   gear.3 gear.4 gear.5 factor(cyl)4 factor(cyl)6 factor(cyl)8
## Mazda RX4              0      1      0            0            1            0
## Mazda RX4 Wag          0      1      0            0            1            0
## Datsun 710             0      1      0            1            0            0
## Hornet 4 Drive         1      0      0            0            1            0
## Hornet Sportabout      1      0      0            0            0            1
## Valiant                1      0      0            0            1            0
\end{verbatim}

In summary, the \texttt{predict()} function is used to apply the dummies
that were created by the \texttt{dummyVars()} function to a new dataset.
It is a way to use the same set of dummies that were created for one
dataset to create dummies for a different dataset that contains the same
categorical variable as the original dataset.

Note that the ``dummyVars()'' function can also be used to create
dummies for multiple categorical variables simultaneously using the
``+'' operator in the formula.

Note that categorical variable should be converted to factor type
before.

\hypertarget{prediction}{%
\subsection{Prediction}\label{prediction}}

\hypertarget{basic-usage}{%
\subsubsection{Basic Usage}\label{basic-usage}}

In R, the \texttt{predict()} function is used to generate predicted
values. It can be used with various types of models (such as linear
regression, logistic regression, decision trees, etc.) and can generate
corresponding predicted values based on input of new data.

Here are the basic steps to use the \texttt{predict()} function:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  First, pass the model object to the \texttt{predict()} function.

  If no more parameters are specified, then the \texttt{predict(model)}
  will return the fitted values of such model. You can image that as
  passing all IV(s) into the parameter \texttt{newdata} to make
  predictions. However, you can get the fitted values through
  \texttt{model\$fitted.values} and \texttt{fitted(fit)}. The folllowing
  three lines of command will give you the completely same result.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{predict}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\NormalTok{fit}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{fitted.values}
\FunctionTok{fitted}\NormalTok{(fit)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Next, pass the new data wanted to predict to the \texttt{predict()}
  function.

  This new data should be a data frame or matrix, with the number of
  \textbf{columns} matching the number of predictor variables used in
  the model.

  In our example presented in ``Logistic Regression'' above, the model
  has four predictor variables. All the four predictors should be
  incorporated into the data frame. In case of problems of variables'
  order, the colnames should correspond with names used in the model.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{glm}\NormalTok{(ln\_yesno }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ age }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathsize }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathscat2 }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathscat3,}
          \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ logisticData,}
          \AttributeTok{family =} \FunctionTok{binomial}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}logit\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For instance, we're to predict a case with \texttt{pathscat\ ==\ 2},
\texttt{pathsize\ ==\ 2.0}, \texttt{age\ ==\ 66} and another case with
\texttt{pathscat\ ==\ 1}, \texttt{pathsize\ ==\ 0.5},
\texttt{age\ ==\ 20}. Accordingly, create the data frame containing the
conditions of the two cases.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{conditions }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{pathscat2 =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{pathscat3 =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{pathsize =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,.}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{age =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{66}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}

\FunctionTok{predict}\NormalTok{(fit,}\AttributeTok{newdata =}\NormalTok{ conditions,}\AttributeTok{type =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}response\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##         1         2 
## 0.1847871 0.2964413
\end{verbatim}

\texttt{predict(model,newdata)} will return a vector containing the
estimated predicted values. If you have multiple predictor variables,
the predicted values will be a matrix, with each row representing a new
predicted value.

\hypertarget{type}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{type}}{type}}\label{type}}

The \texttt{type} parameter in the \texttt{predict()} function in R is
used to specify the type of prediction that you want to generate. The
possible values for the \texttt{type} parameter depend on the type of
model that you are using. The default value of \texttt{type} is
\texttt{response}, which is typically the most appropriate option for
most models.

For example, if you are using a linear regression model, the
\texttt{type} parameter can be set to \texttt{response} or
\texttt{terms}. If you set \texttt{type} to \texttt{response}, the
prediction() function will return the predicted values of the dependent
variable. If you set \texttt{type} to ``terms'', the function will
return the predicted values of the individual terms in the model (i.e.,
the predicted values for each predictor variable).

Similarly, if you are using a logistic regression model, the
\texttt{type} parameter can be set to \texttt{response}, \texttt{terms},
or \texttt{link}. If you set \texttt{type} to \texttt{response}, the
\texttt{predict()} function will return the predicted
\textbf{probabilities} of the binary outcome variable. If you set
\texttt{type} to \texttt{terms}, the function will return the predicted
values of the individual terms in the model. If you set \texttt{type} to
\texttt{link}, the function will return the predicted values on the
\textbf{log-odds} scale.

In generalized linear models (GLMs), such as logistic regression or
Poisson regression, the \texttt{link} scale refers to the scale used in
modeling the relationship between the predictor variables and the
response variable. The link function maps the predicted values to the
scale of the response variable. For example, in logistic regression, the
link function is typically the logit function, which maps the
probability of a binary outcome to the log odds of that outcome.

When you set \texttt{type} to \texttt{link} in the prediction()
function, it returns the predicted values on the link scale, which may
be different from the scale of the response variable.

On the other hand, when you set \texttt{type} to \texttt{response} in
the prediction() function, it returns the predicted values on the scale
of the response variable. For example, in logistic regression, the
predicted values would be the probabilities of the binary outcome.

The choice between \texttt{link} and \texttt{response} depends on the
purpose of the analysis. If you want to compare the predicted values for
different values of the predictor variables on the scale of the response
variable, then \texttt{response} is the appropriate option. If you want
to perform further analysis or transformation on the predicted values,
such as calculating odds ratios or performing hypothesis tests, then
\texttt{link} is the appropriate option.

Back to our example, we can see it more clearly.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{predict}\NormalTok{(fit,}\AttributeTok{newdata =}\NormalTok{ conditions,}\AttributeTok{type =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}response\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##         1         2 
## 0.1847871 0.2964413
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{predict}\NormalTok{(fit,}\AttributeTok{newdata =}\NormalTok{ conditions,}\AttributeTok{type =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}link\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##          1          2 
## -1.4842449 -0.8643023
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{classification-table-1}{%
\subsection{Classification Table}\label{classification-table-1}}

\texttt{confusionMatrix()} is a function from the \texttt{caret} package
in R that creates a confusion matrix and computes various performance
metrics for classification models. The function takes at least two
arguments: \texttt{data} and \texttt{reference}.The \texttt{data}
argument is a vector of predicted class labels, while the
\texttt{reference} argument is a vector of actual class labels. The two
vectors must have the same length.

If necessary, also specify \texttt{positive} and \texttt{dnn}.
\texttt{positive} will tell the factor level that corresponds to a
``positive'' result. If there are only two factor levels, the first
level will be used as the ``positive'' result. \texttt{dnn} reads a
character vector of dimnames for the table.

When you call \texttt{confusionMatrix()} with these arguments, it
produces a confusion matrix that shows the number of true positives,
true negatives, false positives, and false negatives for the
\textbf{classification model}.

Based on this confusion matrix, the function then calculates several
performance metrics, including accuracy, precision, sensitivity (also
known as recall), specificity, F1 score, and Kappa statistic. In
addition to these metrics, the confusionMatrix() function can also
compute various other statistics, such as positive predictive value
(PPV), negative predictive value (NPV), and the area under the ROC curve
(AUC).

More specifically,

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Accuracy: The proportion of correct predictions out of the total
  number of predictions.
\item
  Precision: The proportion of true positives (i.e., correct positive
  predictions) out of the total number of positive predictions.
\item
  Sensitivity/Recall: The proportion of true positives out of the total
  number of actual positives (i.e., the proportion of positive cases
  that were correctly identified).
\item
  Specificity: The proportion of true negatives (i.e., correct negative
  predictions) out of the total number of actual negatives (i.e., the
  proportion of negative cases that were correctly identified).
\item
  F1 score: The harmonic mean of precision and recall, which provides a
  balanced measure of both metrics. It ranges from 0 to 1, with higher
  values indicating better performance.
\item
  Kappa statistic: A measure of agreement between the predicted and
  actual class labels that accounts for the proportion of agreement that
  is expected by chance alone. It ranges from -1 to 1, with higher
  values indicating better agreement.
\item
  Positive predictive value (PPV): The proportion of true positives out
  of the total number of positive predictions.
\item
  Negative predictive value (NPV): The proportion of true negatives out
  of the total number of negative predictions.
\item
  Area under the ROC curve (AUC): A measure of the model's ability to
  distinguish between positive and negative cases across all possible
  thresholds. It ranges from 0.5 (indicating no discrimination) to 1
  (indicating perfect discrimination).
\end{itemize}

Use the example provided above in ``\emph{Logistic Regression}'' to show
the function!

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fit }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{glm}\NormalTok{(ln\_yesno }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ age }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathsize }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathscat2 }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ pathscat3,}
          \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ logisticData,}
          \AttributeTok{family =} \FunctionTok{binomial}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}logit\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{caret}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{confusionMatrix}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{data =} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{round}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{fitted}\NormalTok{(fit),}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)),}
  \AttributeTok{reference  =} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(logisticData}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{ln\_yesno),}
  \AttributeTok{positive =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
  \AttributeTok{dnn =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}predicted\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}actual\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Confusion Matrix and Statistics
## 
##          actual
## predicted   0   1
##         0 846 246
##         1  14  15
##                                           
##                Accuracy : 0.7681          
##                  95% CI : (0.7422, 0.7925)
##     No Information Rate : 0.7672
