\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{RA\_results\_rv }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{reliability}\NormalTok{(athletes,}
                            \AttributeTok{vars =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x4\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x10\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x13\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x39\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x41\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x45\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{),}
                            \AttributeTok{meanScale=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{sdScale=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{corPlot=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{alphaItems=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}
                            \AttributeTok{meanItems=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{sdItems=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{itemRestCor=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}
                            \AttributeTok{revItems=}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x39\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x41\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x45\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{nonadditivity-test}{%
\subsection{Nonadditivity Test}\label{nonadditivity-test}}

Nonadditivity test paves the way for reliability test, which checks
whether the item factor interact with the subject factor. Nonadditivity
test is conducted by \texttt{agricolae::nonadditivity()}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(agricolae)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The data for nonadditivity test should be in \textbf{long-format}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{athletes\_long }\OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ athletes }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} \FunctionTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x39 =} \DecValTok{6}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{x39,}
                       \AttributeTok{x41 =} \DecValTok{6}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{x41,}
                       \AttributeTok{x45=} \DecValTok{6}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{x45) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} 
  \FunctionTok{pivot\_longer}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{cols =} \FunctionTok{everything}\NormalTok{(),}
                    \AttributeTok{names\_prefix =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
                    \AttributeTok{names\_to =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}item\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
                    \AttributeTok{values\_to =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}score\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} 
  \FunctionTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{subj =} \FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(athletes),}\AttributeTok{each =} \FunctionTok{ncol}\NormalTok{(athletes)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For nonadditivity test, first construct a model without interaction
term. Then, the \texttt{nonadditivity()} receives four parameters.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{y}: Answer of the experimental unit, which is the score for
  each item.
\item
  \texttt{factor1}: \textbf{subject} factor (first treatment applied to
  each experimental unit).
\item
  \texttt{factor2}: \textbf{item} factor (second treatment applied to
  each experimental unit).
\item
  \texttt{df}: Degrees of freedom of the experimental error
\item
  \texttt{MSerror}: Means square error of the experimental
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{model\_nonadd }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{lm}\NormalTok{(score }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ subj}\SpecialCharTok{+}\FunctionTok{as.factor}\NormalTok{(item),athletes\_long)}
\NormalTok{MSerror }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{deviance}\NormalTok{(model\_nonadd)}\SpecialCharTok{/}\FunctionTok{df.residual}\NormalTok{(model\_nonadd)}
\NormalTok{res }\OtherTok{=}  \FunctionTok{with}\NormalTok{(athletes\_long, }\FunctionTok{nonadditivity}\NormalTok{(score,subj,item, }\FunctionTok{df.residual}\NormalTok{(model\_nonadd), MSerror))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Tukey's test of nonadditivity
## score 
## 
## P : -55.72362
## Q : 330.7249 
## 
## Analysis of Variance Table
## 
## Response: residual
##                 Df Sum Sq Mean Sq F value   Pr(>F)   
## Nonadditivity    1    9.4  9.3888  6.7735 0.009307 **
## Residuals     2486 3445.9  1.3861                    
## ---
## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{icc}{%
\subsection{ICC}\label{icc}}

Note that package ``\texttt{psych}'' is used here. ICC is also available
in other packages (``\texttt{psy}'', ``\texttt{irr}''), but the
functions would be used quite differently.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(psych)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Note that whenever you use the data to carry reliability analysis, the
reversely-coded items should be corrected.

In \texttt{ICC()}, specify parameter \texttt{check.keys\ =\ TRUE} to
reverse those items that do not correlate with total score (with
alerting message and reversed items). Note that this is not done by
default.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ICC\_results }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{ICC}\NormalTok{(athletes,}\AttributeTok{check.keys =}\NormalTok{ T)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Some items were negatively correlated with total scale and were automatically reversed.
##  This is indicated by a negative sign for the variable name.
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
##  reversed items
##    x39 x41 x45
\end{verbatim}

\texttt{ICC()} can be designated to an object, and the most important
returned values are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{results}: A matrix of 6 rows and 8 columns, including the
  ICCs, \(F\) test, \(p\) values, and confidence limits
\item
  \texttt{stats}: The anova statistics (converted from lmer if using
  lmer)
\end{itemize}

Types of ICC are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  ICC1: single random factor
\item
  ICC2: two-way random factor
\item
  ICC3: two-way mixed factor
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ICC\_results}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{results  }
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                          type       ICC        F df1  df2           p
## Single_raters_absolute   ICC1 0.1030658 1.919272 311 2184 7.30497e-17
## Single_random_raters     ICC2 0.1115472 2.097898 311 2177 1.26596e-21
## Single_fixed_raters      ICC3 0.1206760 2.097898 311 2177 1.26596e-21
## Average_raters_absolute ICC1k 0.4789692 1.919272 311 2184 7.30497e-17
## Average_random_raters   ICC2k 0.5011019 2.097898 311 2177 1.26596e-21
## Average_fixed_raters    ICC3k 0.5233324 2.097898 311 2177 1.26596e-21
##                         lower bound upper bound
## Single_raters_absolute   0.07308939   0.1382257
## Single_random_raters     0.08104023   0.1471864
## Single_fixed_raters      0.08910136   0.1575172
## Average_raters_absolute  0.38681209   0.5620130
## Average_random_raters    0.41366008   0.5799578
## Average_fixed_raters     0.43900148   0.5993178
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ICC\_results}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{stats  }\CommentTok{\#the ANOVA results}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##           subjects       Judges    Residual
## df    3.110000e+02 7.000000e+00 2177.000000
## SumSq 8.002308e+02 2.578900e+02 2670.108979
## MS    2.573089e+00 3.684144e+01    1.226508
## F     2.097898e+00 3.003765e+01          NA
## p     1.265960e-21 7.229148e-40          NA
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{survival-analysis}{%
\section{Survival Analysis}\label{survival-analysis}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(survival)}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(survminer)}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggfortify)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Dataset ``lung'' in package ``survival'' is of interest, which contains
the information about survival in patients with advanced lung cancer
from the North Central Cancer Treatment Group. Variables include:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  time: Survival time in days
\item
  status: censoring status \texttt{1=censored}, \texttt{2=dead}
\item
  sex: \texttt{Male=1} \texttt{Female=2}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{attach}\NormalTok{(lung)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{survival-model}{%
\subsection{Survival Model}\label{survival-model}}

\hypertarget{survival-object}{%
\subsubsection{Survival Object}\label{survival-object}}

\texttt{survival::Surv()} creates a survival object, with censored cases
marked with ``\texttt{+}'' following up censoring time and death cases
simply showing the death time. The result is usually used as a response
variable in a model formula. Two most important parameters:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{time} :For right censored data, this is the follow up time.
  For interval data, the first argument is the starting time for the
  interval.
\item
  \texttt{event}: The status indicator, normally \texttt{0=alive},
  \texttt{1=dead}. Other choices are \texttt{TRUE/FALSE}
  (\texttt{TRUE\ =\ death}) or \texttt{1/2} (\texttt{2=death}).
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{survobj }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{Surv}\NormalTok{(time, status}\SpecialCharTok{==}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{overall-model-1}{%
\subsubsection{Overall Model}\label{overall-model-1}}

Use \texttt{survival::survfit(formula,data)} to fit the model. For the
\emph{overall} survival analysis, just specify the null hypothesis with
\texttt{Surv\textasciitilde{}1}, i.e., NO groups.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sfit }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{survfit}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{Surv}\NormalTok{(time, status}\SpecialCharTok{==}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{data=}\NormalTok{lung)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The fitted survival model itself will tell you the results, with
observations, events, median and 95\% CI.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sfit}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Call: survfit(formula = Surv(time, status == 2) ~ 1, data = lung)
## 
##        n events median 0.95LCL 0.95UCL
## [1,] 228    165    310     285     363
\end{verbatim}

If more details are needed, i.e., the instantaneous events and estimates
of survival, std.err, 95\% CI, just \texttt{summary(sfit)}. Moreover, if
\texttt{time} is designated, then the \texttt{summary()} function will
return the \emph{estimated} results.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(sfit,}\AttributeTok{time =} \DecValTok{200}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Call: survfit(formula = Surv(time, status == 2) ~ 1, data = lung)
## 
##  time n.risk n.event survival std.err lower 95% CI upper 95% CI
##   200    144      72     0.68  0.0311        0.622        0.744
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{grouped-model}{%
\subsubsection{Grouped Model}\label{grouped-model}}

If grouping is of interest, specify it in the formula with
\texttt{Surv\textasciitilde{}group}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sfit\_sex }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{survfit}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{Surv}\NormalTok{(time,status}\SpecialCharTok{==}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{sex)}
\NormalTok{sfit\_sex}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Call: survfit(formula = Surv(time, status == 2) ~ sex)
## 
##         n events median 0.95LCL 0.95UCL
## sex=1 138    112    270     212     310
## sex=2  90     53    426     348     550
\end{verbatim}

It's ideal that the 95\% CI of groups doesn't intersect, and a
significant result can be obtained instantly. However, that's often not
the case; take a step further and use Cox regression to see exactly.

\hypertarget{cox-regression}{%
\subsection{Cox Regression}\label{cox-regression}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{coxph}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{Surv}\NormalTok{(time, status}\SpecialCharTok{==}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{sex)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Call:
## coxph(formula = Surv(time, status == 2) ~ sex)
## 
##        coef exp(coef) se(coef)      z       p
## sex -0.5310    0.5880   0.1672 -3.176 0.00149
## 
## Likelihood ratio test=10.63  on 1 df, p=0.001111
## n= 228, number of events= 165
\end{verbatim}

where ``\texttt{exp(coef)}'' is the HR (hazard ratio)， which measures
the relative risk between two groups.

Theoretically, it's better only with binary variables in Cox regression.
However, for categorical variables with more than 2 levels, use
``\texttt{as.factor(cat\_var)}'' with ``\texttt{model\ =\ TRUE}'' to
generate corresponding dummy variables. For continuous variables,
they'll be included into the regression model as usual, but the model
will be plotted with expected value of continuous variables.

The simpler version of such comparison can be accomplished by
``\texttt{survival::survdiff()}''. Both of the functions have the same
formula. From ``\texttt{survdiff()}'', we can get the intermediate
results when computing the log rank, or the chi-square test. However,
this wouldn't return the coefficient. Jointly speaking, the combined
results of two functions emphasize the fact that the log rank test
(named Mantel-Haenszel test), is a chi-square test in essence.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{surv\_diff }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{survdiff}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{Surv}\NormalTok{(time, status}\SpecialCharTok{==}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{sex)}
\NormalTok{surv\_diff}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Call:
## survdiff(formula = Surv(time, status == 2) ~ sex)
## 
##         N Observed Expected (O-E)^2/E (O-E)^2/V
## sex=1 138      112     91.6      4.55      10.3
## sex=2  90       53     73.4      5.68      10.3
## 
##  Chisq= 10.3  on 1 degrees of freedom, p= 0.001
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{surv-plots}{%
\subsection{Surv Plots}\label{surv-plots}}

Use ``\texttt{ggsurvplot(model,data)}'' to visualize the survival
analysis. Note that if the ``\texttt{data}'' wasn't passed into
\texttt{survfit()}, an error will be raised.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{ggsurvplot}\NormalTok{(sfit\_sex,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ lung,}
           \AttributeTok{conf.int=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{pval=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }
           \AttributeTok{risk.table=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}abs\_pct\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# risk table below the Kaplan{-}Meier curve(s)}
           \AttributeTok{legend.labs=}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Male"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Female"}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{legend.title=}\StringTok{"Sex"}\NormalTok{,}
           \AttributeTok{title=}\StringTok{"Kaplan{-}Meier Curve for Lung Cancer Survival"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-220-1.pdf}

Moreover, the parameter ``\texttt{fun}'' is quite useful, which will
define a transformation of the survival curve.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  ``pct'': for survival probability in percentage, \textbf{by default}.
\item
  ``event'': plots cumulative events, \(f(y)=1-y\).
\item
  ``cumhaz'': plots the cumulative hazard function, \(f(y)=-\log(y)\).
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{ggsurvplot}\NormalTok{(sfit\_sex,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ lung,}
           \AttributeTok{conf.int=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{pval=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }
           \CommentTok{\# risk.table=\textquotesingle{}abs\_pct\textquotesingle{},}
           \AttributeTok{legend.labs=}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Male"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Female"}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{legend.title=}\StringTok{"Sex"}\NormalTok{,}
           \AttributeTok{fun =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}event\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-221-1.pdf}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{ggsurvplot}\NormalTok{(sfit\_sex,}\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ lung,}
           \AttributeTok{conf.int=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{pval=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }
           \CommentTok{\# risk.table=\textquotesingle{}abs\_pct\textquotesingle{},}
           \AttributeTok{legend.labs=}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Male"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Female"}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{legend.title=}\StringTok{"Sex"}\NormalTok{,}
           \AttributeTok{fun =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}cumhaz\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-222-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{clustering}{%
\section{Clustering}\label{clustering}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(parameters)}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"factoextra"}\NormalTok{) }
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{hierarchical-clustering}{%
\subsection{Hierarchical Clustering}\label{hierarchical-clustering}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{automobile }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{import}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"data13{-}01.xlsx"}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} 
\NormalTok{  dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}type\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}price\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}engine\_s\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}horsepow\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
           \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}wheelbas\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}length\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}curb\_wgt\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
           \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}fuel\_cap\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}mpg\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} \FunctionTok{drop\_na}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} \FunctionTok{scale}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## ✔ Successfully imported: 157 obs. of 27 variables
\end{verbatim}

Use ``\texttt{hclust()}'' from base R and transmit a
\textbf{dissimilarity} structure produced by ``\texttt{dist()}''.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{distMat }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{dist}\NormalTok{(automobile, }\AttributeTok{method =} \StringTok{"euclidean"}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# Euclidean distance matrix}
\NormalTok{hc }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{hclust}\NormalTok{(distMat)  }\CommentTok{\# hierarchical clustering, using the default method}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Use ``\texttt{plot(hclust\_obj)}'' to visualize the hierarchical
clustering. Note that you should specify ``\texttt{hang\ =\ -1}'' to
ensure all nodes touch the same horizontal line and looks decent. If the
sample isn't that large, set ``\texttt{labels\ =\ TRUE}'' (by default)
to see the corresponding observations.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(hc,}\AttributeTok{labels=}\ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{hang =} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-226-1.pdf}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(hc,}\AttributeTok{labels=}\ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-226-2.pdf}

Moreover, you can draw colored dendrogram with grouping.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{fviz\_dend}\NormalTok{(hc,}\AttributeTok{k=}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{rect =}\NormalTok{ T)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-227-1.pdf}

Also, you can also check how many members for each cluster and make a
frequency table.Note that the grouping might change if we change cluster
number.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{memberLabels }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{cutree}\NormalTok{(hc,}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{table}\NormalTok{(memberLabels)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## memberLabels
##  1  2  3 
## 71 71 10
\end{verbatim}

Then, we can get the centers of cluters by ``\texttt{mean}'' summary
statistics. Use ``\texttt{aggregate()}'' to achieve that.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{x}: usually a data frame.
\item
  \texttt{by}: a list of grouping elements, each \textbf{as long} as the
  variables in the data frame x. The elements are coerced to factors
  before use.
\item
  \texttt{FUN}: a function to compute the summary statistics which can
  be applied to all data subsets.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{aggregate}\NormalTok{(automobile,}\FunctionTok{list}\NormalTok{(memberLabels),mean)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##   Group.1       type      price   engine_s   horsepow   wheelbas      width
## 1       1 -0.4362472 -0.4985325 -0.7231739 -0.6579479 -0.6259158 -0.6459818
## 2       2  0.5201409  0.1193177  0.5017194  0.3293009  0.6601116  0.5979645
## 3       3 -0.5956452  2.6924245  1.5723270  2.3333938 -0.2427902  0.3409229
##        length   curb_wgt   fuel_cap        mpg
## 1 -0.65105171 -0.7853229 -0.7200528  0.6620751
## 2  0.65553921  0.7236353  0.6270241 -0.5592928
## 3 -0.03186123  0.4379815  0.6605042 -0.7297543
\end{verbatim}

Moreover, we can use Silhouette plot to check whether some cases are
outliers.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(cluster)}
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{silhouette}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{cutree}\NormalTok{(hc,}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{), distMat))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-230-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{non-hierarchical-clustering-k-means}{%
\subsection{Non-Hierarchical Clustering
(K-Means)}\label{non-hierarchical-clustering-k-means}}

\hypertarget{computation}{%
\subsubsection{Computation}\label{computation}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{athlete }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{import}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"data13{-}02.xlsx"}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\NormalTok{ dplyr}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{select}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## ✔ Successfully imported: 10 obs. of 4 variables
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{x}: numeric matrix-like data
\item
  \texttt{centers}: either the number of clusters, say kk, or a set of
  initial (distinct) cluster centres. If a number, a random set of
  (distinct) rows in x is chosen as the initial centres.
\item
  \texttt{iter.max}: the maximum number of iterations allowed.
\item
  \texttt{nstart}: if centers is a number, how many random sets should
  be chosen. Trying several random starts is often recommended.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{kmean\_results }\OtherTok{=} \FunctionTok{kmeans}\NormalTok{(athlete, }\AttributeTok{centers =} \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }
                       \AttributeTok{iter.max =} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{nstart =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\texttt{kmeans()} returns an object of class ``\texttt{kmeans}'', a list
with at least the following components:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{cluster} : A vector of integers (from 1:k) indicating the
  cluster to which each point is allocated.
\item
  \texttt{centers}: A matrix of cluster centres.
\item
  \texttt{totss}: The total sum of squares.
\item
  \texttt{withinss}: Vector of within-cluster sum of squares, one
  component per cluster.
\item
  \texttt{tot.withinss} :Total within-cluster sum of squares,
  i.e.~sum(withinss).
\item
  \texttt{betweenss}: The between-cluster sum of squares,
  i.e.~\texttt{totss}-\texttt{tot.withinss}.
\item
  \texttt{size}: The number of points in each cluster.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{kmean\_results}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## K-means clustering with 4 clusters of sizes 2, 2, 3, 3
## 
## Cluster means:
##         x1       x2       x3
## 1 120.5000 19.00000 44.50000
## 2 124.5000 20.00000 44.50000
## 3 121.0000 17.00000 41.66667
## 4 121.6667 18.33333 43.00000
## 
## Clustering vector:
##  [1] 2 4 3 2 4 1 3 4 3 1
## 
## Within cluster sum of squares by cluster:
## [1] 1.000000 1.000000 2.666667 1.333333
##  (between_SS / total_SS =  88.4 %)
## 
## Available components:
## 
## [1] "cluster"      "centers"      "totss"        "withinss"     "tot.withinss"
## [6] "betweenss"    "size"         "iter"         "ifault"
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{visualization-2}{%
\subsection{Visualization}\label{visualization-2}}

Use \texttt{factoextra::fviz\_cluster(object,\ data)}. For more details
about useful parameters, see the help document. Remember that
\texttt{object} and \texttt{data} are two most indispensable parameters.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{fviz\_cluster}\NormalTok{(kmean\_results, }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ athlete)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-234-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{no.-of-clusters}{%
\subsection{No.~of Clusters}\label{no.-of-clusters}}

\hypertarget{within-cluster-ss}{%
\subsubsection{Within-Cluster SS}\label{within-cluster-ss}}

Looking for the ``elbow'' where the SS starts to slow down its drop.
Here suggests 2 or 3.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{fviz\_nbclust}\NormalTok{(automobile, kmeans, }\AttributeTok{method =} \StringTok{"wss"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{print.summary=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-235-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{silhouette-method}{%
\subsubsection{Silhouette Method}\label{silhouette-method}}

Looking for the value that sets the highest average silhouette width,
which indicates the optimal number of clusters.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{fviz\_nbclust}\NormalTok{(automobile, kmeans, }\AttributeTok{method =} \StringTok{"silhouette"}\NormalTok{,}\AttributeTok{print.summary=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-236-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{majority-rule}{%
\subsubsection{Majority Rule}\label{majority-rule}}

\texttt{n\_clusters()} is the main function to find out the optimal
numbers of clusters present in the data based on the maximum consensus
of a large number of methods.Note that the \texttt{n\_clusters()}
function will do the normalization automatically (by default).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{n\_clusters}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(automobile))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # Method Agreement Procedure:
## 
## The choice of 2 clusters is supported by 8 (27.59%) methods out of 29 (Elbow, Silhouette, Ch, Ratkowsky, Mcclain, SDindex, Mixture (VEV), Mixture (EEV)).
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{n\_clusters}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(automobile)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{psy-stat-II-2023_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-237-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{two-step-clustering}{%
\subsubsection{Two Step Clustering}\label{two-step-clustering}}

Two-Step Clustering refers to using hierarchical methods first to obtain
the number of clusters and their centroids. Then use these as inputs to
run non-hierarchical analysis.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# two step clustering in R, not required}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(prcr)}
\NormalTok{d }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ pisaUSA15}
\NormalTok{m3 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{create\_profiles\_cluster}\NormalTok{(d,}
\NormalTok{                              broad\_interest, enjoyment, instrumental\_mot, self\_efficacy,}
                              \AttributeTok{n\_profiles =} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(m3)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}
